Waters v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Corrections


No. 3452 C.D. 1985, No. 2530 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


97 Pa. Commw. 283; 509 A.2d 430; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2175


February 25, 1986, Submitted on Briefs  
May 15, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Stephen P. Waters v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Corrections; Charles H. Zimmerman, Superintendent, Graterford State Correctional Institute, and Ms. Young, State Law Librarian for Graterford, and in case of Stephen P. Waters v. Charles H. Zimmerman, Superintendent, Graterford State Correctional Institution; Curry Shaw, Property Officer; Thomas D. Stachelek, Deputy for Treatment; Stephen R. Lucach, Deputy for Operations; David Diguglelmo, Classification and Treatment Manager; Donald Vaughn, Deputy Superintendent, and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Bureau of Corrections.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections sustained. Petitions denied and dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought writs of mandamus to compel respondent department of corrections and officials to issue petitioner writing implements and stationary free of charge and certain articles so that petitioner could pursue various lawsuits which petitioner had pending.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate requested that respondent department of corrections issue petitioner paper and postage free of charge and provide petitioner with a typewriter, personal law books, and other effects. Respondent's policy was to provide materials free of charge to indigent inmates and respondent denied the request because petitioner had funds to pay for the material. Petitioner commenced mandamus actions to compel respondent to comply on the basis that respondent's policy was unconstitutional, and respondent filed preliminary objections claiming that petitioner had an adequate remedy at law and that mandamus was inappropriate because the actions of which petitioner complained were discretionary. The court agreed and held that mandamus was not the proper procedure for testing the constitutionality of a policy because its purpose was not to establish rights but to enforce those already established. The court also held that in reviewing petitioner's action as an appeal from a determination of a government unit, the action had to be dismissed because the determination was not a final order as petitioner had not exhausted all administrative remedies prior to seeking judicial review.


OUTCOME: Respondent department of correction's preliminary objections to petitioner inmate's actions in mandamus challenging the constitutionality of respondent's policy in refusing to issue writing material to petitioner on the basis that petitioner was not indigent were sustained and petitioner's action was dismissed on the grounds that mandamus was inappropriate and that petitioner had not exhausted his administrative remedies.

Gardner v. Peoples


No. 28 T.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


95 Pa. Commw. 600; 506 A.2d 479; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1999


October 9, 1985, Argued  
March 17, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in the case of John W. Gardner, C. Richard Logan, William C. Stouffer, Commissioners of the County of Blair, Pennsylvania, and Robert A. Grove, Controller of the County of Blair, Pennsylvania v. Thomas G. Peoples, Jr., President Judge of the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Pennsylvania, individually and on behalf of the Judges of the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Pennsylvania, and Alfred E. Wegemer, Sheriff of Blair County, Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Judgment rendered for judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county commissioners and county controller filed an application for plenary jurisdiction and relief against respondent judges to obtain relief from a restraining order preventing mandatory retirement at age 70 from applying to court criers and court attendants.


OVERVIEW: The court denied the application for plenary jurisdiction and relief filed against respondent judges by petitioner county commissioners and county controller, who sought to obtain relief from a restraining order preventing mandatory retirement at age 70 from applying to court criers and court attendants. Under separation of powers, it found, the power to hire, supervise, and fire court personnel lay exclusively with the courts, and the mandatory retirement rule interfered with this power. It also noted that 16 P.S. § 1620, which conferred upon the county salary board the power to fix the compensation of all employees paid from the county treasury and upon the county commissioners the sole responsibility to represent the judges of the court of common pleas in collective bargaining negotiations involving such employees, explicitly stated that that these responsibilities did not affect the hiring, discharging, and supervision rights of judges over their employees.


OUTCOME: Finding that imposing mandatory retirement upon court employees violated separation of powers, the court denied the application for plenary jurisdiction and relief filed against respondent judges by petitioner county commissioners and county controller, who sought to obtain relief from a restraining order preventing mandatory retirement at age 70 from applying to court criers and court attendants.

Grosser v. L.E. Smith Glass Co.


Nos. 1351 C.D. 1983, 1352 C.D. 1983, 1517 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


95 Pa. Commw. 450; 505 A.2d 1093; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1970


October 9, 1985, Argued  
March 6, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County in the cases of Clarence Grosser v. L.E. Smith Glass Co. and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, No. 5 of 1983; William C. Wells v. Duraloy Blaw-Knox and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, No. 7 of 1983, and Steve A. Keto v. L.E. Smith Glass Company and Pennsylvania Manufacturers' Association Insurance Company, No. 6930 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employers challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which allocated liability for appellee claimants' injuries between appellants and appellee commonwealth.


OVERVIEW: Appellee claimants filed occupational disease claims for silicosis under 77 P.S. § 1401 of the Occupational Disease Act (Act). Appellant employers argued that that § 1401 was unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, and the trial court erred in allocating liability between appellants and appellee commonwealth. The court held that appellee claimants' compensation was to be paid as provided by the Act. The court found that the only section of the Act that referred to determining liability for compensation was 77 P.S. § 1408(a), which required appellants and appellee commonwealth to be jointly liable for compensation awarded because of disability or death caused by silicosis. The court affirmed because the constitutional section insured that an employee was compensated for his work-related injuries, not that an employer would know who would be potential claimants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that allocated liability for appellee claimants' injuries because, pursuant to statute, both appellant employers and appellee commonwealth were jointly liable for compensation because of disability caused by silicosis.

DISPOSITION: Reversed.

The court reversed the order that directed reinstatement of the employee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that directed reinstatement of petitioner's employee to his position in security. The court found that petitioner employer did not act improperly when it dismissed employee because employee's position was one that involved public safety, and additional accommodation would not have been reasonable.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


95 Pa. Commw. 178; 505 A.2d 357; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1927


September 25, 1985, Submitted  
February 19, 1986, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the State Board of Nurse Examiners in case of In The Matter of the Suspension or Revocation of License No. RN-221306-L, issued November 16, 1977, to Ann Marie Rafferty, dated December 28, 1981.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nurse challenged an order from respondent State Board of Nurse Examiners (Pennsylvania), revoking her nursing license for unauthorized actions of removing a comatose patient from a respirator. Petitioner argued that 49 Pa. Code § 21.11(a)(4) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner nurse appealed an order from respondent State Board of Nurse Examiners, which revoked her nursing license. Petitioner disconnected her comatose patient from his respirator to perform an unauthorized evaluation of spontaneous respiration, leaving her patient after noting that he had no heartbeat, failing to perform external cardiac resuscitation and artificial respiration, failing to immediately call the code team, and failing to attach the cardiac monitor strip to her patient's chart. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of 49 Pa. Code § 21.11(a)(4) as unconstitutionally vague. This section required nursing care actions which promoted, maintained, and restored the well-being of individuals. The court rejected petitioner's challenge because the regulation was not vague with respect to her conduct. The Pennsylvania Supreme Court upheld respondent's findings that petitioner's actions were unauthorized, contraindicated, and a serious deviation from acceptable nursing practice. Therefore, the order of respondent was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The revocation of petitioner nurse's nursing license by respondent state board of nurse examiners was affirmed because petitioner's conduct towards a patient deviated from the acceptable nursing practice. Woloszyn v. Commonwealth, Unemployment Compensation Bd. of Review


No. 3067 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


95 Pa. Commw. 164; 504 A.2d 984; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1922


November 20, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
February 14, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied December 29, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review in case of In Re: Claim of Frank A. Woloszyn, No. B-234558.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged an order of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a referee's decision that petitioner was ineligible for benefits under 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 804(c).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee challenged an order of the unemployment compensation board of review, which affirmed a referee's decision that he was ineligible for benefits. The board found that petitioner accumulated less than the minimum 18 credit weeks necessary to establish financial eligibility under 43 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 804(c). Petitioner did not receive credit for 30 weeks of work that was determined to be participation in a federally assisted work relief or work training program. Petitioner argued that the board capriciously disregarded his testimony that his employment at the federally funded employer did not constitute participation in a work training program. The court affirmed, holding that the board did not capriciously disregard petitioner's testimony. The court found that petitioner worked as a foreman for the employer at $ 3.85 per hour, above the minimum wage, and that further information as to the nature of his position or duties was irrelevant in determining his eligibility for benefits. There was no merit to petitioner's claim that the classifications created under the statute were unconstitutionally overbroad as applied to claimants in his position.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the board, which affirmed a referee's decision that petitioner employee was ineligible for unemployment benefits. The board properly denied benefits because petitioner's job as a foreman was excluded from the classification of employment under the statute because the job was part of a federally assisted work training program.

Brewster v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


94 Pa. Commw. 277; 503 A.2d 497; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2072


December 11, 1985, Argued  
January 22, 1986, Decided

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order from appellee, the Department of Transportation, that revoked appellant's driver's license for five years pursuant to the habitual offender provisions of the Motor Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1542. Appellant sought review and argued that the section of the code was unconstitutional because it mandated that an accelerated rehabilitative disposition (ARD) program constituted an offense for purposes of determining habitual offender status. Drogowski v. Commonwealth


No. 2938 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


94 Pa. Commw. 205; 503 A.2d 104; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2061


October 10, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
January 15, 1986, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied September 10, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. James F. Drogowski, No. SA 506 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision of appellee Department of Transportation that revoked his driver's license upon a determination that appellant was a habitual offender, pursuant to 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 1542.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Department of Transportation revoked appellant driver's license upon a determination that appellant was a habitual offender, 42 Pa. Const. Stat. § 1542. Specifically, the revocation followed appellant's convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol, driving without lights to avoid identification or arrest, and fleeing or attempting to elude a police officer. The trial court affirmed the revocation. The appellate court also affirmed, holding that the constitutional limitations against cruel and unusual punishment, Pa. Const. art. I, § 13, did not apply to revocation proceedings. In so ruling, the appellate court noted that revocation proceedings were civil in nature. Further, respondent's actions were not discretionary, but were mandated by statute. Further, the appellate court held that the level of appellant's culpability was not relevant, as revocation was not punishment of appellant, but protection for the public. Lastly, the appellate court held that the three separate offenses were not merged into a single act merely because the conduct occurred during one continuous episode.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's order that affirmed a decision of appellee Department of Transportation, revoking appellant driver's license because the constitutional limitations against cruel and unusual punishment were not applicable, appellant's level of culpability was not relevant, and appellant's three convictions, even though they occurred during one continuous episode, were separate acts.

Osser v. Philadelphia


No. 14 T.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


94 Pa. Commw. 116; 503 A.2d 466; 1986 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2071


September 12, 1985, Argued  
January 9, 1986, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in case of Maurice Osser v. City of Philadelphia, No. 2352 August Term, 1976.



DISPOSITION: Order of lower court dismissing motion for judgment on pleadings is affirmed. Case remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a former city commissioner convicted of mail fraud and other offenses, sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) denying his motion for judgment on the pleadings, which alleged that appellee city's denial of pension benefits was unauthorized pursuant to the First Class Cities Act and appellee's ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a former city commissioner, was convicted of mail fraud, conspiracy, and endeavoring to obstruct a criminal investigation. Appellee city denied appellant's application for pension benefits, and appellant brought an action in assumpsit against appellee. The trial court denied appellant's motion for judgment on the pleadings, and appeals were taken which resulted in the state Supreme Court remanding the case to the court for a determination of issues certified for appeal. The court affirmed the trial court's order denying appellant's motion for judgment on the pleadings, and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court held that appellee was not precluded under the First Class Cities Act, 53 P.S. §§ 13431- 13449, or the First Class City Home Rule Act, 53 P.S. §§ 13101-13157, from passing the ordinance under which appellant's benefits were denied. The court also held that appellant's actions on which the criminal convictions were based arguably constituted grounds for disqualifying the pension benefits under the city ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying the motion of appellant, a former city commissioner, for judgment on the pleadings, and appellant's action was remanded to the trial court. The court held that appellee city was not precluded from passing the ordinance under which appellant's pension benefits were denied, and appellant's criminal offenses arguably constituted grounds for disqualifying his receipt of pension benefits.

 

844 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Ekin v. Board of Comm'rs


No. 3393 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


93 Pa. Commw. 528; 502 A.2d 303; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1446


October 11, 1985, Argued  
December 17, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted 11/18/86; (512 Pa. 410; 517 A.2d 539).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County in the case of Louis D. Ekin et ux., et al. v. Board of Commissioners of the County of Allegheny et al., Miscellaneous No. 22, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners and tax authorities in one county, sought review of an order in favor of respondent tax authorizes in an adjacent county, by the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County (Pennsylvania), which ruled that the location of a boundary line between two counties was established by the United States Coast and Geodetic Survey of 1934. The properties were to be taxed in the respective counties in accordance to that line.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, property owners and tax authorities in one county, filed an action against respondent tax authorities in an adjacent county to determine the boundary line between two counties. The court designated the lower court as a neutral court under 16 P.S. § 302(a), to hear the boundary dispute. The lower court after a hearing determined that the boundary line was the line found by the United States Coast and Geodetic Survey of 1934, pursuant to an agreement between the two counties. On appeal, the court affirmed because the previous authority to readjust county boundaries where the existing county line became inconvenient or improper was abrogated by Pa. Const. art. 9, § 8. Under that article, the general assembly had until April 23, 1970 in which to enact uniform legislation to establish a procedure for altering the boundaries of municipalities. The failure of the general assembly to so act abrogated all such pre-existing legislation that dealt with alteration of municipal boundaries and left initiative and referendum as the sole method of changing boundaries. The court held the properties should be taxed in the respective counties in accordance with the 1934 line.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's finding against appellants, property owners and tax authorities in one county, and in favor of respondent tax authorities in an adjacent county and held the United States Coast and Geodetic Survey of 1934 established the boundary between the two counties. The properties were to be taxed in the respective counties in accordance with the 1934 line.

 

845 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Phila. Elec. Co. v. Pa. Public Util. Com


Nos. 44 C.D. 1984, 3561 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


93 Pa. Commw. 410; 502 A.2d 722; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1434


May 8, 1985, Argued  
December 11, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission in case of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission et al. v. Philadelphia Electric Company, No. R-822291.



DISPOSITION: Order in 44 C.D. 1984 affirmed. Order in 3561 C.D. 1983 vacated in part and affirmed in part. Case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, electric company and consumer advocate, sought review of a decision of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which denied proposed rate increases designed to increase petitioner electric company's annual operating revenues by $ 228,233,000, and permitted petitioner electric company to file a tariff designed to produce an increase in annual operating revenues of $ 143,518,000.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (PUC) denied petitioner electric company's proposed tariff for rate increases, but allowed petitioner electric company to submit a tariff designed to produce a smaller annual revenue increase. Petitioner electric company and petitioner consumer advocate appealed the order. The court found that the PUC did not err in disallowing certain expenses associated with the installation of pollution control equipment because the expenses were incurred during a test year for which petitioner electric company had already been granted a rate increase, the expenses had not been included in the projected expenses for that year, and the expenses were neither recurring nor so extraordinary as to justify recovery now. The court also found that the PUC did not err by allowing the inclusion in the rate base of expenses for nuclear fuel because nuclear fuel was neither a plant, nor equipment, nor a facility as used in 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1315. The court remanded the decision of the PUC for a definitive finding of whether inclusion in the rate base of expenses for system reinforcement was proper pursuant to § 1315 as safety improvements.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the decision of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (PUC) for definitive finding of whether expenses for system reinforcement by petitioner electric company were properly included in the rate base as safety improvements. The court affirmed in all other respects the order of the PUC that permitted petitioner electric company to file a tariff designed to produce an increase in annual operating revenues of $ 143,518,000.

 

846 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Reider v. Commonwealth, Bureau of Correction


No. 70 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


93 Pa. Commw. 326; 502 A.2d 272; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1417


October 9, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
December 6, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of William D. Reider v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Bureau of Correction, et al.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections overruled in part and affirmed in part. Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition for review addressed to the court's original jurisdiction that sought relief from actions by respondents, Pennsylvania Bureau of Correction and superintendent of state correctional institution, which denied petitioner prerelease status pursuant to 61 P.S. §§ 1051-4.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, Pennsylvania Bureau of Correction and superintendent of state correctional institution, denied petitioner inmate prerelease status under 61 P.S. §§ 1051-4. Petitioner filed a petition for review to the court's original jurisdiction and contended that respondent's denial to petitioner of prerelease status violated petitioner's constitutional rights. The court held that it had original jurisdiction over the action against the Commissioner of the Bureau of Correction (commissioner) and would exercise its jurisdiction over the superintendent of the state correctional institution, as ancillary to the claims against the commissioner. The court held that petitioner had no constitutional right to prerelease status and that evaluation of the inmate for prerelease programs was subjective and was a matter of administrative discretion. The Pennsylvania statute did not violate petitioner's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court held that it had jurisdiction to consider petitioner inmate's petition to review respondents', Pennsylvania Bureau of Correction and superintendent of state correctional institution, decision to deny prerelease status to petitioner. The court ruled that petitioner did not have a constitutional or statutory liberty right to prerelease status.

 

847 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Milewski v. Commonwealth


No. 93 T.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


93 Pa. Commw. 120; 500 A.2d 1261; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1393


October 10, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
November 22, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument and/or Reargument En Banc Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Felix Steve Milewski, Nos. 795 and 839 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Lower court orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Erie County (Pennsylvania) for a conviction for violating minimum size gill net regulations promulgated by the Pennsylvania Fish Commission under authority of Section 2903(e) of the Fish and Boat Code, 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2903(e).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of violating minimum size gill net regulations promulgated by the state Fish Commission (commission) under the Fish and Boat Code, 30 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2903(e). Appellant challenged the conviction on grounds of insufficient evidence, conviction under the wrong statutory section, and vagueness of the statutory and regulatory framework under which appellant was prosecuted. The court affirmed the judgment. The commission's interpretation of its own regulation, 58 Pa. Code § 63.3(a), was entitled to controlling weight, and the particular rule used to measure mesh size of appellant's gill nets was not required to be proven as a necessary element for proving a violation of the statute. The evidence established the accuracy of flexible rules used to measure appellant's gill nets and the commission's compliance with its own regulations. The court also found that appellant waived argument that he was prosecuted under the wrong statutory section by failing to present it in either the pre-trial proceedings or during trial. Lastly, the court found no ambiguity in the regulation's description of what conduct was prohibited, and the regulation was not void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment of sentence for a conviction for violating the minimum size gill net regulations. The court held that the Fish Commission's (commission) interpretation of its own regulation was entitled to controlling weight and the evidence established the accuracy of the flexible rules used to measure appellant's gill nets and the commission's compliance with its own regulations.

 

848 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


County of Allegheny v. Commonwealth


No. 636 C.D. 1985


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


93 Pa. Commw. 112; 500 A.2d 1267; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1394


October 9, 1985, Argued  
November 22, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Judgment entered by, Stay granted by County of Allegheny v. Commonwealth, 517 Pa. 65, 534 A.2d 760, 1987 Pa. LEXIS 843 (1987)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in the case of County of Allegheny v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections sustained and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county commenced a declaratory judgment proceeding in the court (Pennsylvania) against respondent commonwealth requesting an order that respondent provide all funds necessary for the functioning of all parts of the unified court system and, specifically, all funds necessary for the functioning of petitioner's court of common pleas. Respondent filed a demurrer.


OVERVIEW: In considering petitioner county's request for a declaratory judgment against respondent commonwealth, the court found that the legislature had committed the financial underpinning of the county judicial system to the counties, and the court was compelled to hold that only the legislature could make a change in funding. The court concluded that the matter was non-justiciable. While the court had the authority and the duty to declare legislative enactments unconstitutional where they offended the state constitution, the court had no power to fashion a judicial remedy requiring the legislature to fund the county court system when that obligation had been imposed on the counties. The court held that petitioner lacked standing to raise the issue of varying salaries on behalf of employees who were not parties to the action, and the payment of different salaries to employees in different counties with similar occupational titles had a rational basis for differentiation, because the work and duties of an employee in the courts of a metropolitan area would necessarily vary from those of a similar employee in a less populated area. The court was compelled to sustain respondent's demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court sustained all preliminary objections and respondent commonwealth's demurrer. Petitioner county's request for a declaratory judgment was denied. There was no justiciable controversy, and the court was without the power to fashion the remedy requested by petitioner.

Lower Allen Citizens Action Group, Inc. v. Lower Allen Township Zoning Hearing Bd.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-profit corporation challenged the order the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed its appeal of its challenge to a proposed expansion of quarrying operations conducted by appellee corporation. OVERVIEW: The court denied appellee corporation's motion to quash, for lack of standing, appellant non-profit corporation's challenge to a proposed expansion of quarrying operations conducted by appellee corporation. The court held that appellee township did not abuse its discretion nor act in an arbitrary or capricious manner injurious to the public interest when it adopted the amendments to the ordinance providing for a mineral recovery district.


OUTCOME: The motion to quash for lack of standing was denied because appellant non-profit corporation had standing because it was the legal successor-in-interest to the party to the proceedings before appellee board. The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant corporation's challenge to the proposed expansion because there was substantial evidence rebutting appellant's argument that the expansion would be harmful to the area's development.

Sullivan v. County of Bucks


Nos. 710 C.D. 1985, 711 C.D. 1985, 712 C.D. 1985, 713 C.D. 1985, 714 C.D. 1985, 715 C.D. 1985, 716 C.D. 1985, 717 C.D. 1985, 718 C.D. 1985, 719 C.D. 1985, 1843 C.D. 1984, 1844 C.D. 1984, 1845 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


92 Pa. Commw. 213; 499 A.2d 678; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1309


June 5, 1985, Argued  
October 11, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County in case of Daniel J. Sullivan et al. v. County of Bucks et al., No. 84-8358, and in case of North Wales Water Authority et al. v. Neshaminy Water Resources Authority et al., No. 84-3273.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants county, a water authority, and a taxpayer, challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which entered a verdict in favor of appellees, electric company and two water authorities, in a suit interpreting agreements to construct a water supply facility.


OVERVIEW: An electric company, water authorities, and two counties entered into agreements to construct a facility to supply water. One county (breaching county) attempted to terminate its agreement to construct parts of the project. Appellants, county, a water authority, and a taxpayer, brought suit to challenge the project and the trial court upheld the agreements. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that appellees, electric company and the water authorities, had standing as third party beneficiaries to enforce the agreements. The court held that the agreements did not allow appellee electric company to exercise governmental powers in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 31, as appellee electric company was only authorized to have limited input as to the implementation of a county's proprietary contracts and was not permitted to interfere with discretionary functions. The court found that the breaching county was not at liberty to void the agreements merely because of public sentiment against the project and the court ordered specific performance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the agreement between an electric company, water authorities, and two counties for construction of a water supply facility served the public interest and was not ultra vires. The court ordered specific performance to remedy a county's breach of the agreement.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mining company challenged the order of the Environmental Hearing Board of Pennsylvania, which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent state agency and dismissed petitioner's appeal from respondent's denial of its variance request.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mining company acquired the rights to 700 acres of coal, and acquired the surface estate in 71 of those acres. White Haven Borough v. Workmen's Comp. Appeal Bd. (cunningham)


No. 1558 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


92 Pa. Commw. 123; 498 A.2d 1003; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1196


July 2, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
October 1, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board in case of George W. Cunningham v. White Haven Borough, No. A-82493.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the city and its insurer, appealed the order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their petition for modification of the Notice of Compensation Payable under which payments were made to the claimant for injuries he sustained while serving in petitioner city's volunteer fire department.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, the city and its insurer, entered into an agreement for workmen's compensation payments for a claimant who was injured while serving in the volunteer fire department. The claimant received payments for a continuing partial disability based upon an average weekly wage as provided for in § 601 of the Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 1031. Despite these agreed upon payments, petitioners filed a petition to modify alleging that § 601 was unconstitutional and the amount of payments should be modified. Petitioners appealed the decision of respondent workmen's compensation board dismissing their motion, and the court affirmed. The court held that there was no novelty in a wage computation formula that reflected a justifiable social purpose such as § 601. The court concluded that it saw in § 601, as with other provisions for computing a wage that may not be directly related to the injured employee's actual earnings, a provision that was rationally related to a legitimate government interest. The court found no constitutional defect in § 601 that called for a ruling to strike down its wage provision, and affirmed respondent's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent workmen's compensation board, as the applicable statute's wage provision was not unconstitutional, and the amount of compensation payable to the claimant, who was injured while serving in petitioner city's volunteer fire department, did not need to be modified.

Chemclene Corp. v. Commonwealth Dep't of Environmental Resources


No. 1470 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


91 Pa. Commw. 316; 497 A.2d 268; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1155


June 5, 1985, Argued  
August 22, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Pennsylvania Environmental Hearing Board in case of Chemclene Corporation et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources, Docket No. 81-168-M.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hazardous waste transporters petitioned for review of an adjudication of the Environmental Health Board (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their constitutional challenges to 35 P.S. § 6018.505(e) requiring them to file a collateral bond with the department of environmental regulation as a condition for state hazardous waste transport licenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant hazardous waste transporters petitioned for review of an adjudication of the environmental health board which dismissed their constitutional challenges to § 505(e) of the solid waste management act, 35 P.S. § 6018.505(e), which required them to file a collateral bond with the department of environmental regulation (department) as a condition for state hazardous waste transport licenses. The court disagreed with appellant's contention that § 505(e) was preempted by the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act of 1980 or by the Resource Conservation Recovery Act as their express language disclaimed preemptive intent. The court held that equal protection was not violated when collateral bonds were imposed upon transporters but not on owners of hazardous waste as transporters and owners were not similarly circumstanced. The court finally rejected appellants' argument that the department's unpublished documents used to determine bond assessments were void for having been promulgated without compliance with the documents law as the documents were interpretive and rather than legislative. Accordingly the adjudication of the board was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The adjudication was affirmed because there was no evidence that congress intended to preempt state regulation of the transportation of hazardous waste, equal protection was not violated by bonding requirements and department's unpublished documents used for bond assessments were interpretive and therefore exempt from the rulemaking procedure of the commonwealth documents law.

Cons. Stat. §§ 8541-8564.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured party sued appellee city in trespass for damages on account of injuries sustained when appellant was attacked in a parking lot owned by appellee. Judgment on the pleadings was entered in favor of appellee based on immunity pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8541-8564. The court found that there was a reasonable possibility that appellant could successfully amend the complaint to allege that appellee had notice or could be charged with notice of a dangerous condition with specific reference to lighting of the parking lot.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which upheld the suspension of his operating privileges pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(b), for refusing to submit to a breathalyzer test.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was stopped during a routine traffic check and refused to submit to a breathalyzer test. Appellant's operating privileges were suspended under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(b). The trial court affirmed the suspension and found that a traffic stop was not an arrest in the strict sense and when a driver refused to submit to a breathalyzer test, the driver's license could be suspended. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that suspended appellant's driving privileges. The court found that the trial court's findings were supported by substantial evidence. Because the traffic check was methodical and left nothing to the officer's discretion, appellant driver's constitutional rights had not been violated.

Chester Upland School Dist. v. Commonwealth


No. 2924 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 464; 495 A.2d 981; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 983


May 6, 1985, Argued  
July 18, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Chester Upland School District, The Board of School Directors of Chester Upland School District, Donald Tonge and Peggy Charleston v. The Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, The Honorable Richard Thornburgh, Governor of Pennsylvania, The Honorable LeRoy Zimmerman, Attorney General of Pennsylvania, The Pennsylvania Department of Justice, Pennsylvania Department of Education, and The Honorable Robert Wilburn, Secretary of Education.



DISPOSITION: Motion for summary judgment denied. Preliminary objections sustained. Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, commonwealth and various officials, filed preliminary objections and demurred, and petitioners, school district and board of directors, moved for summary judgment in petitioners' action for declaratory judgment, which challenged the constitutionality of an amendment to the Public School Code, 24 P.S. § 11-1106.


OVERVIEW: An amendment to 24 P.S. § 11-1106 prohibited school districts from requiring employees to reside within the district as a condition of employment. Petitioner school district had a policy of requiring non-professional employees to live in the district. Petitioners, school district and board of directors, brought a declaratory judgment action under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7531-7541 against respondents, commonwealth and various officials, seeking a declaration that the amendments were unconstitutional and did not apply to the non-professional employees. Petitioners alleged that employees who had been threatened with discharge under its policy had threatened to bring legal action against petitioners under the amendments. Petitioners did not join those employees in the action. The court sustained respondents' preliminary objections and dismissed the complaint. There was no justiciable controversy. Respondents had not taken any action to enforce the amendments against petitioners. Under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7540 a declaratory judgment action did not lie where interested parties who could be affected by the judgment, the employees threatened with termination, were not joined.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of respondents, commonwealth and various officials, were sustained and the complaint for declaratory judgment of petitioners, school district and board of directors, was dismissed. Petitioners' motion for summary judgment was denied. The matter did not present an actual case or controversy, where there had been no steps towards enforcement, and necessary parties had not been joined.

Rice v. Board of Directors of Easton Area School Dist.


No. 1601 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 447; 495 A.2d 984; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 984


June 4, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
July 18, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County in case of Robert W. Rice v. The Board of Directors of the Easton Area School District, No. 1983-C-330.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant discharged custodian sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his objection to the decision of appellee school board, terminating his employment pursuant to the Public School Code, 24 P.S. § 5-514. Appellant contended that his dismissal for improper conduct was not supported by substantial evidence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant discharged custodian was charged with making harassing calls to another custodian, which, if proven, would be in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5504. Appellee school board discharged appellant and offered a hearing, at which appellant presented no evidence. Appellee affirmed the termination and appellant's initial appeal was dismissed. Appellant argued that his termination for making harassing phone calls was not supported by substantial evidence. The court, in affirming the lower court's dismissal of the initial appeal, disagreed and cited extensive testimony in the record to that effect. Appellant also argued that, even if he made the calls, that conduct was insufficient to support his dismissal. The court disagreed. 24 P.S. § 5-514 of the Public School Code specifically delegated to appellee the authority to dismiss an employee for "improper conduct" and the court determined that appellant's conduct fell within that definition. As to the question of that term being unconstitutionally vague, and to a procedural challenge, the court held that those challenges had not been properly preserved and were waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, which dismissed appellant discharged custodian's objection to appellee school board's termination of his employment. The court found substantial evidence to support that he had made harassing phone calls to another employee, which was conduct contemplated within the definition of the dischargeable offense of "improper conduct."

Appleton Papers, Inc. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Labor & Industry


No. 337 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 399; 495 A.2d 662; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1007


May 6, 1985, Argued  
July 16, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Department of Labor and Industry, Employer Accounts Review Board, in case of In Re: 1982 Contribution Rate Appeal of Appleton Papers, Inc. (formerly Germaine Monteil Cosmetiques Corporation), Employer Account No. 99-1981.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner company appealed an order of respondent Department of Labor and Industry (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of the Office of Employment Security, denying petitioner's contribution rate appeal. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of the unemployment compensation law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 781(d)(2), and attacked the classification, allowing the contribution rate of a successor-in-interest, as arbitrary.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner company had an established employer contribution account and it merged with another company but retained its old name. Petitioner's contribution rate after the merger was determined under the Unemployment Compensation Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 781(d)(2). Petitioner applied for a redetermination of its contribution rate, after the transfer, requesting that its former subsidiary's experience record and reserve account balance be considered. The Office of Employment Security denied the application and respondent Department of Labor and Industry affirmed. Petitioner challenged the constitutionality of § 781(d)(2) on equal protection grounds and argued that the statute was not rationally related to the legislative goal that a successor-in-interest would be assigned only one tax rate for the entire transfer year. The court, affirming respondent's order, held that the classification in the statute bore a rational relationship to the legislative goal. The court stated that because petitioner was a successor-in-interest before the transfer, two different tax rates could apply during the same calendar year. The court rejected petitioner's claim of unconstitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent department of labor and industry, upholding petitioner company's contribution rate. The court concluded that the classification in the statute bore a rational relationship to the legislative goal, such that where the successor-in-interest was an employer before the transfer a potential for two applicable rates existed. The court rejected petitioner's claim of unconstitutionality.

Jamieson v. Commonwealth, Pennsylvania Bd. of Probation & Parole


No. 5 Miscellaneous Docket No. 4


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 318; 495 A.2d 623; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 977


April 2, 1985, Submitted on Briefs  
July 2, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Joseph Jamieson v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole.



DISPOSITION: Motion denied. Order of Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed a petition for writ of mandamus with the court (Pennsylvania), which sought to compel the Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole to recompute the maximum term expiration dates of his two sentences for robbery by assault by force and rape. Petitioner argued that the former 19 Pa. Stat. § 897 was unconstitutional as applied to him.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner was convicted and sentenced for robbery by assault by force and rape, the lower court aggregated petitioner's sentences into a single 15- to 30-year sentence. Petitioner filed a petition for writ of mandamus with the court challenging the constitutionality of former 19 Pa. Stat. § 897, which mandated the aggregation of his sentences. The court held that petitioner could not establish legal rights by mandamus because the purpose of mandamus was to enforce those rights already established; however, the court asserted its appellate jurisdiction over the action. The court held that former § 897 did not involve a fundamental right, nor did convicted offenders who received consecutive sentences at the same time in the same court qualify as a "discrete and insular group" in need of extraordinary protection; as such, where § 897 bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state objective, namely that of effective administration of the corrections and parole system while also preserving the integrity of consecutive sentences imposed by the trial court, petitioner's equal protection challenge had to fail. The court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order determining the maximum term expiration dates of petitioner's two sentences, holding that the former statute mandating the aggregation of petitioner's two sentences into a single sentence did not involve a fundamental right; as such, where the statute bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state objective, petitioner's equal protection challenge had to fail.

 

861 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth, Pennsylvania Labor Relations Bd. v. Vecchia


No. 816 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 235; 494 A.2d 1151; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1264


March 13, 1985, Argued  
June 26, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied Granted 4/29/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in the case of Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board v. Michael A. Della Vecchia, Recorder of Deeds of Allegheny County and The County of Allegheny, No. S.A. 431 of 1983.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant labor relations board challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) in a labor action, where the trial court had reversed an order from appellant, which had concluded that appellee county recorder of deeds had committed an unfair labor practice.


OVERVIEW: Appellant labor relations board challenged an order from the trial court reversing an order of appellant, which concluded that appellee county recorder of deeds had committed an unfair practice in violation of the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1101.1201(a)(1) and (5), by refusing to submit an employment dispute to binding arbitration pursuant to the terms of a memorandum of understanding between appellee and the service employees international union. The court reversed. The agreement was reflective of the dichotomy of control established by case law where it contained provisions related solely to the issues of hiring, firing and supervising employees. These were matters regarding which the county commissioners could not effectively negotiate because these matters were not within their control. However, the court held that once a public employer entered into an agreement on matters of managerial prerogative, where there was no obligation to bargain, the employer will be bound. Appellee was bound by the agreement to arbitrate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the trial court, reversing appellant labor relations board's order that appellee county recorder of deeds had committed an unfair labor practice, because appellee, once committed to an agreement on matters of managerial prerogative was thereafter bound by that agreement.

Aquarian Church of Universal Service v. County of York


No. 3170 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 290; 494 A.2d 891; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1091


May 6, 1985, Argued  
June 25, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County in the case of The Aquarian Church of Universal Service v. County of York, West York Borough, West York Area School District, York County Board of Assessment Appeals and Tax Claim Bureau, No. 81-S-4564.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania) that vacated an injunction restraining the tax sale of appellant's property and dismissed appellant's complaint in equity.


OVERVIEW: After appellant church failed to pay property taxes, appellee county scheduled a tax sale. Appellant filed a complaint in equity, asserting that as a church and as a charity, its property was exempt by statute and not subject to assessment. The trial court initially enjoined the sale of appellant's property, but upon the filing by appellee of exceptions, vacated the injunction and dismissed the complaint. Appellant sought review and argued that the exemptions for churches and charities were self-executing and that it should not have to follow the administrative procedure to obtain an exemption. The court disagreed and held that where the legislature had provided a statutory remedy that was mandatory and exclusive, equity was without power to act in relief of a party who failed to pursue that remedy. In addition, the court held that absent a challenge to the constitutionality of a statute or of official action thereunder, equity had no jurisdiction to restrain the collection of taxes. The court held that appellant had failed to state a substantial constitutional issue and affirmed the order dismissing the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order vacating an injunction and dismissing appellant church's appeal because it held that absent a substantial constitutional challenge, equity had no jurisdiction to restrain the collection of taxes.

Ridley v. Ridley Arms, Inc.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


90 Pa. Commw. 143; 494 A.2d 870; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1085

June 14, 1985, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County in the case of Ridley Arms, Inc. and Leonard Klorfine, t/a Ridley Brook Associates v. Township of Ridley, No. 79-4268.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff apartment complex challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which refused to order refund of trash collection fees paid to defendant township. Defendant township also challenged the portion of order which required defendant to reimburse plaintiff for trash collection fees paid by plaintiff to a private trash collector.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff apartment complex brought action challenging waste removal ordinance which required plaintiff to pay for township trash collection costs when plaintiff utilized a private trash collector instead of the defendant township to haul away trash. The trial court found the ordinance constitutional and refused to order a refund of the costs paid by plaintiff to the township. The trial court did, however, order the defendant township to reimburse plaintiff the amount paid by plaintiff for private trash collection. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's order requiring the defendant to reimburse plaintiff, and affirmed the trial court's order in all other respects. The court ruled that plaintiff voluntarily paid for private trash collection costs and was not entitled to a refund in absence of filing a claim for same.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order requiring the defendant township to reimburse plaintiff apartment complex for waste collection costs paid by plaintiff for private waste collection. The court affirmed the constitutionality of the waste collection ordinance and the order of the trial court which refused to grant plaintiff reimbursement of the waste collection costs paid by it to the defendant.

 

864 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Balent v. Wilkes-Barre


No. 2180 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


89 Pa. Commw. 578; 492 A.2d 1196; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1053


December 10, 1984, Argued  
May 31, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County in the case of Joseph J. Balent and George Barto v. City of Wilkes-Barre, No. 2167-C of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) dismissed appellant building owners' request for the appointment of a board of viewers to assess damages because it concluded that appellee city's demolition of their building was an exercise of police power and was not compensable. Appellants sought review of the judgment contending that the demolition was a taking.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, owners of a building, sought review of a judgment from the trial court dismissing their petition for the appointment of a board of viewers to assess damages after appellee city demolished their building, which was seriously damaged by fire. The trial court concluded that the demolition was an exercise of police power, and was not compensable. The court affirmed and held that appellee's demolition of appellants' building was an exercise of police power, rather than eminent domain. The evidence showed that appellants failed to make the required repairs to bring the building up to the building code and that the building was dangerous to the public. In addition, vandalism, rotting wood, and wind damage made the property a hazard. Appellee's demolition did not constitute a taking because it did not appropriate the property for public use, but rather it demolished it under its police power. Accordingly, the court held that no compensation was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that appellee city's demolition of appellant owners' building was an exercise of police power rather than eminent domain. Appellants failed to make required repairs after their building was seriously damaged by fire and the building became dangerous to the public. Appellee did not appropriate appellants' property for public use, and thus no compensation was required.

Croasdale v. Dauphin County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 1597 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


89 Pa. Commw. 409; 492 A.2d 793; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1049


October 16, 1984, Argued  
May 22, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County in the case of Joan E. Croasdale v. Dauphin County Board of Assessment Appeals, No. 291 S 1984.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) sustaining the validity of the reassessment program initiated by appellee assessment board. Appellant sought to prevent appellee from collecting a higher levy pursuant to the General County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5020-402(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer brought an action against appellee assessment board to contest its novel reassessment program, and appellant sought to prevent appellee's consequent collection of a greater levy. The court reversed the trial court order that affirmed appellee's program. The court found evidence indicating that appellee's program would geographically cover all of the county and that about 90 percent of all properties would be reassessed. The court rejected appellee's argument that because only 10 percent of those properties would be assessed at a precise market value and the rest of them would not be, no county-wide reassessment would occur. The court determined that appellee's program constituted a de facto county-wide reassessment triggering the delay provisions of the General County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5020-402(a), as they applied to this third class county. Therefore, the court declined to address appellant's contention of unconstitutional discrimination by appellee. The trial court was reversed because the Law § 5020-402(a) prohibited any levies by appellee on county-wide reassessments until all the assessments were 100 percent completed using the proper procedures.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order that affirmed the validity of the reassessment program developed by appellee assessment board because, although appellee only intended to specifically assess 10 percent of the properties, appellee's plan imposed a county-wide reassessment on all properties. Therefore, the court enabled appellant to prevent appellee from collecting the higher levy because its plan triggered the statutory delay provision.

Kar Kingdom, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1146 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 364; 489 A.2d 972; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1223


December 10, 1984, Argued  
March 25, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County in case of Kar Kingdom, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Board of Middletown Township, No. 82-2651-13-1.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellee zoning board's order rejecting an appeal to it from a citation issued by a district justice, and refusing an application for a sign variance. Appellant claimed that there was a timely appeal filed to appellee, that certain evidence should not have been allowed, and that he was entitled to a variance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner sought review of the lower court's order affirming a decision of appellee zoning board, which found that appellant was not entitled to a variance for a sign on his property, for allowing certain evidence to be heard, and rejecting his appeal from a citation issued by a district justice. Appellant filed an appeal of the citation order and a request for a variance. The court found that appellant failed to file an appeal from the zoning officer's order to remove the sign within 30 days from its issuance. The issuance by a district justice of a citation was not a decision of the zoning officer, and its propriety was not a matter appellee had the power to decide; therefore, it was an unappealable order. Matters of evidence or argument were not offered and, therefore, were properly admitted. Appellant did not show that the zoning regulations uniquely burdened his property or that those regulations were excessive or without relationship to the interests they were intended to promote. The lower court's order was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The order affirming appellee zoning board's decision that appellant property owner was not entitled to a variance for a sign, and rejecting his appeal from a citation issued by a district justice was affirmed, because the issuance by a district justice of a citation was an unappealable order, and the zoning regulation did not uniquely burden appellant's property, and was not excessive or arbitrary.

American Trucking Assos. v. Scheiner


No. 160 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


87 Pa. Commw. 418; 489 A.2d 269; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1493


December 11, 1984, Argued  
March 21, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of American Trucking Associations, Inc., Old Dominion Freight Line, Inc., and Anderson Trucking Service, Inc., individually and on behalf of a class of interstate motor carriers similarly situated v. James I. Scheiner, Secretary of the Department of Revenue of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, LeRoy S. Zimmerman, Attorney General of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and Daniel F. Dunn, Commissioner of the State Police of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objection sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, trucking association and interstate motor carriers, filed an original action for equitable and declaratory relief and sought a refund of taxes paid for identification markers pursuant to the Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(b), against respondents, the secretary of the Department of revenue and other associated individuals.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, trucking association and motor carriers, filed this action, and sought a refund of taxes paid by petitioner motor carriers for identification markers pursuant to the Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(b). In a prior action, the court held that § 2102(b) was unconstitutional because it facially discriminated against foreign motor carrier vehicles. In this action, respondents, secretary of the Department of revenue and other associated individuals, preliminarily objected that the court did not have jurisdiction because petitioners had not exhausted their administrative remedies. The court sustained respondents' preliminary objection and held that pursuant to 72 P.S. § 503, because § 2102(b) was declared unconstitutional, petitioners were required to petition the Board of Finance and Revenue for a refund. Further, the court held that petitioners could not invoke equitable jurisdiction to circumvent their administrative remedy by alleging class status. Finally, under the Declaratory Judgments Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7541(c)(2), the court could not grant relief because the proceeding was within the exclusive jurisdiction of another tribunal.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objection raised by respondents, secretary of the Department of revenue and other associated individuals. The court held that petitioners, trucking association and motor carriers, failed to exhaust their administrative remedies because they did not apply to the Board of Finance and Revenue for a refund as required by the statute.

In re Ross Township Election Dist. Reapportionment


No. 521 C.D. 1985


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 233; 489 A.2d 297; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 895


March 11, 1985, Argued  
March 13, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted 7/23/86.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in the case of In Re: Ross Township Election District Reapportionment, Misc. Docket No. 6, April Term, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. First plan reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff electors appealed a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which accepted the report of a reapportionment commission and thereby reapportioned a township from nine to seven wards. Plaintiffs contended the trial court erred in its earlier order setting aside the township's first reapportionment plan.


OVERVIEW: This action involved a township's adoption of a reapportionment plan. Plaintiff electors filed a petition contesting the plan. After an evidentiary hearing, the trial court set aside the plan. Thereafter, defendant reapportionment commission devised a second reapportionment plan that divided the township into seven wards. The trial court confirmed the second plan and thereby reapportioned the township from nine to seven wards. Plaintiffs appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in its earlier order setting aside the township's first reapportionment plan. The appeals court reversed the trial court decision which accepted the appointed reapportionment commission's report as final. The court also reversed the earlier decision setting aside the township's reapportionment plan and reinstated the original plan. The court reasoned that the record was devoid of evidence that defendant had access to any data regarding socioeconomic indicators and the considerations of financial status, race, and religion did not intrude upon the formulation of the plan. Hence, because plaintiffs did not establish a prima facie case of discrimination, defendant was not required to justify the plan.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the trial court decision setting aside the township's second reapportionment plan and reinstated the original plan because the original plan was valid. The court ruled that plaintiffs were required to produce a prima facie case of discrimination before defendant was required to justify the plan.

Sultanik v. Board of Supervisors


No. 1737 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 214; 488 A.2d 1197; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 898


December 11, 1984, Argued  
March 13, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in the case of Bronia Sultanik v. Board of Supervisors of Worcester Township and Worcester Township, No. 81-20163.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owner sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed her appeal from a decision of the board of supervisors authorizing appellant's requested residential use of an agriculturally zoned area but limiting it to 19 percent of the proposed number of units, on not more than 39 percent of the proposed residential site area.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owner filed curative amendment proceedings under the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code (MPC), 53 P.S. §§ 10609.1, 11004, concerning an agriculturally-zoned tract of land. The board of supervisors authorized appellant's requested residential use of the tract, but limited it to 19 percent of the proposed number of units, on not more than 39 percent of the proposed residential site area. The trial court dismissed an appeal from the board's decision, and appellant sought review, contending that the board's decision was void on a commingling ground. The court held that the factual proceedings were invalid because of the commingling of functions. Attorneys from the same firm represented appellee township's case against appellant's claim and also served as counsel to the board. Further, the court held that the limited relief proposed by appellee township could not stand as conclusive. The relief had to be governed by the provisions of MPC § 1011(2), 53 P.S. § 11011(2). Thus, the court vacated the trial court's decision and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of the trial court, which dismissed an appeal from a decision of the board of supervisors limiting appellant's requested residential use of an agriculturally-zoned area to 19 percent of the proposed number of units. The board's decision was void on commingling grounds, and relief had to be fashioned pursuant to Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code.

C.C. Collings & Co. v. Commonwealth


Nos. 1584 C.D. 1982, 1585 C.D. 1982, 1027 C.D. 1982, 1028 C.D. 1982, 2125 C.D. 1981, 2403 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 184; 488 A.2d 1187; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1364


October 16, 1984, Argued  
March 6, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Orders of the Board of Finance and Revenue in the cases of C. C. Collings & Co., Inc., Docket Nos. 5797 and 5798; A. E. Masten & Company, Inc., Docket Nos. R-4541 and R-4865, and Commonwealth Securities & Investments, Inc. (formerly Henry Fisher & Co., Inc., d/b/a Henry Fisher Municipals), Docket Nos. R-4521A and R-4625.



DISPOSITION: Appeals sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers sought review from orders of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which sustained the action of the Department of Revenue in disallowing a deduction, for the purposes of the corporate net income tax, of profits from the sale of obligations issued by respondent, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayers challenged the action of the Department of Revenue in disallowing the deduction, for the purposes of the corporate net income tax, of profits from the sale of obligations issued by respondent, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The issue presented was the legality of the Department's alteration of long-standing tax policy by mandating the inclusion in the tax base of profits from the sale of Commonwealth municipal obligations. Petitioners contended that 72 P.S. § 4752-1 forbade the inclusion. The court held that notwithstanding the abstract statement of purpose set forth in 72 P.S. § 7402 to exact from businesses payment for the exercise of the privilege of doing business in the Commonwealth, the corporate net income tax was a tax on income. There was no economic consequence that followed from the use of the particular words, "privilege of doing business" and a focus on that formalism merely obscured the question. Accordingly, the court ruled in favor of petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of petitioner taxpayers in their appeal from the disallowance of deductions of profits from the sale of obligations issued by respondent, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, for the purposes of the corporate net income tax. The court held that notwithstanding the statement of purpose to exact from businesses payment for the privilege of doing business in the Commonwealth, the corporate net income tax was a tax on income.

Kratz v. Board of Comm'rs


No. 1966 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 108; 488 A.2d 670; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 865


February 1, 1985, Argued  
March 5, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in the case of Jay Forrest Kratz and Jeffrey F. Kratz v. Board of Commissioners of Upper Gwynedd Township, Donald A. Hamme, Jr., Barbara Anne E. W. Hamme, Paul D. Lukens, Carey D. Lukens, Intervenors, No. 82-00084.



DISPOSITION: Appeal quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, board of commissioners, sought review pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 762(a)(4) of an order from the Montgomery County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which reversed appellant's dismissal of appellees' challenge to an ordinance that excluded mobile home parks.


OVERVIEW: Appellees challenged a zoning ordinance on the ground that it unconstitutionally excluded mobile home parks and requested a curative amendment that would have allowed appellees to develop their parcel of land as a mobile home park. Appellant board of commissioners dismissed appellee's challenge. Appellees sought review from the trial court, which reversed appellant's decision and remanded back to appellant for consideration of an appropriate remedy acceptable to both parties. The trial court retained jurisdiction during the pendency of the proceedings. Appellant argued on appeal that jurisdiction was conferred upon the court pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 762(a)(4). On review, the court found that jurisdiction under that section was limited to appeals from final orders of the courts of common pleas. The court held that an adjudication had to dispose of the entire case, end the litigation, or effectively put the litigant our of court in order for it to be considered a final and appealable order. The court quashed the appeal, holding that appellate jurisdiction of an interlocutory order could not have been assumed, even with the consent of the parties.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal because it lacked jurisdiction. The court held that the order appealed from was interlocutory because it neither disposed of the entire case, ended the litigation, nor effectively put the parties out of court.
