Smith v. Commonwealth


No. 1922 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


87 Pa. Commw. 643; 488 A.2d 1174; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 852


September 11, 1984, Argued  
February 26, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Mary P. Smith, individually and as Administratrix of the Estate of Frank Smith v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania; Walter Baran, Secretary of General Services; The Department of General Services; Robert C. Wilburn, Secretary of Budget and Administration and Richard Thornburgh, Governor.



DISPOSITION: Motion of plaintiff denied. Motion of Commonwealth granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow filed a motion for summary judgment in the trial court (Pennsylvania) arguing that the State Employees Group Life Insurance Act (Act), 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780.2, which reduced life insurance benefits for employees 65 years of age or older, had no rational basis, and, thus, it violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 26. Defendant agency filed a cross motion arguing § 780.2 was valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff widow's husband was a state employee until his death at the age of 69. Defendant agency provided group life insurance coverage for state employees under the State Employees Group Life Insurance Act (Act) and paid life insurance benefits for any insured employee over the age of 65 at a 35 percent reduction than that provided to younger employees. Plaintiff filed a motion for summary judgment seeking the balance of the maximum benefit allowable. Defendant filed a cross motion for summary judgment, which the court granted. The provision that reduced benefits for older employees within the Act, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780.2 did not violate the equal protection clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 26. The court held that the age classification rationally furthered a legitimate state purpose to reduce life insurance costs based upon statistical evidence of five-year age cost comparisons which showed older employees' insurance costs increased significantly. The court found further justification for the different treatment because § 780.2 was in compliance with the Age Discrimination in Employment Act's, 29 U.S.C.S. § 631 guidelines.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant agency's cross motion for summary judgment because the provision of the State Employees Group Life Insurance Act that reduced life insurance benefits for employees 65 years of age and older furthered a legitimate state purpose of reducing the costs of insurance associated with insuring such employees. Thus, it did not violate the equal protection clause of the United States and Pennsylvania constitutions.

Fitzgerald v. Philadelphia


No. 2181 C.D. 1983, No. 2444 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


87 Pa. Commw. 482; 487 A.2d 485; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 830


June 5, 1984, Argued  
February 11, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in case of Bernard Fitzgerald, on behalf of himself and a class of all persons similarly situated v. City of Philadelphia, No. 3526 May Term, 1978, dated September 3, 1982 and July 29, 1983.



DISPOSITION: Motion to quash denied. Orders affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant class representative challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that limited damages to a nominal amount in a class action suit that challenged the constitutionality of procedures for hearings on the validity of tows under appellee city's towing ordinance, Philadelphia, Pa., Code § 12-2406. Appellee challenged the award to appellant of counsel fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellant class representative filed a class action on behalf of individuals whose vehicles were towed pursuant to Philadelphia, Pa., Code § 12-2406. The trial court ruled that appellee city's towing ordinance's provision for a delayed hearing was unconstitutional, but determined that class members were not entitled to refunds of towing or storage fees, and therefore limited damages to a nominal amount. The trial court also granted appellant counsel fees. The appellate court affirmed the limitation on damages because it was not error to refuse to refund towing and storage fees when the constitutional defect pertained only to the collection fees, and the loss of use of those fees during the delayed period before a hearing was held. There was no defect in the hearing procedures and thus class members suffered no harm by the ultimate adjudicated loss of paid fees. However, the appellate court reversed the grant of counsel fees to appellant. Neither 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1726(1), nor 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2503 authorized counsel fees when the trial court decertified the class due to the nominal damage award and no common class fund was created from which to pay counsel fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the order that limited appellant class representative's damages to a nominal amount, because there was no right to a refund of towing and storage fees to class members when the constitutional defect in appellee city's towing ordinance related to the collection of fees without a prompt hearing. The court reversed the award of counsel fees granted appellant because there was no basis for the award.

American Trucking Assos. v. Bloom


No. 315 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


87 Pa. Commw. 345; 487 A.2d 468; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 820


December 11, 1984, Argued  
February 5, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of American Trucking Associations, Inc. and Old Dominion Freight Line, Inc. and Anderson Trucking Service, Inc., individually and on behalf of a class of interstate motor carriers v. Robert K. Bloom, Secretary of the Department of Revenue of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and LeRoy S. Zimmerman, Attorney General of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and Daniel F. Dunn, Commissioner of the State Police of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Exceptions overruled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a class action suit, respondents, the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, Attorney General and State Police Commissioner, filed exceptions to an order that entered judgment in favor of petitioners, out-of-state motor carriers, and declared that the identification marker fee requirement of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(b) violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, out-of-state motor carriers, filed a class action suit against respondents, the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue, Attorney General and State Police Commissioner, challenging the constitutionality and seeking to enjoin enforcement of the identification marker fee requirement of the Vehicle Code, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(b). The trial judge found that the requirement violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, and respondents filed exceptions. The court overruled respondents' exceptions and affirmed the trial judge's order, holding that § 2102(b) facially discriminated against foreign-registered motor carrier vehicle operators and in favor of Pennsylvania-registered motor carrier vehicle operators in violation of the Commerce Clause. The court found that the legislature intended to, and actually did, exclude Pennsylvania-registered motor carriers from the financial impact of the marker levy by deeming the marker fee to be part of and included in the Pennsylvania registration fee, thereby imposing the full tax burden on foreign-registered motor carriers while exempting Pennsylvania-registered motor carriers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial judge's finding that the identification marker fee requirement of the Vehicle Code was an unconstitutional violation of the Commerce Clause because it discriminated against interstate commerce.

 

875 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


American Trucking Assos. v. Bloom


No. 267 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


87 Pa. Commw. 379; 487 A.2d 465; 1985 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 819


December 11, 1984, Argued  
February 5, 1985, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of American Trucking Associations, Inc., Tri-State Motor Transit Company, Interstate Van Lines, Inc., Interstate Truck Leasing, Inc. and Astro Container Company, individually and on behalf of a class of interstate motor carriers similarly situated v. Robert K. Bloom, Secretary of the Department of Revenue of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, LeRoy S. Zimmerman, Attorney General of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and Daniel F. Dunn, Commissioner of the State Police of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Petitioners' motion for summary judgment granted. Legislation declared invalid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner truckers filed an action in the court, which challenged the constitutionality of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9902, requested an injunction against its enforcement, and sought a refund of axle taxes that were paid under it.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner truckers claimed that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9902 was unconstitutional, requested that its enforcement be enjoined, and sought a refund of the taxes that were paid under it. The court granted petitioners' summary judgment motion, denied the commonwealth's cross-motion for summary judgment, declared that the legislation was invalid, and awarded the requested tax refund. The court held that the examination of § 9902 in conjunction with the simultaneously enacted registration fee reductions demonstrated that § 9902 violated the Commerce Clause under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 because the axle tax that was assessed under that statute facially discriminated against interstate commerce. The court held that the registration fee reductions almost always offset assessments, and that those reductions virtually insulated operators of locally but not foreign, registered vehicles from the levy's financial impact. The court also characterized that disproportionate impact as facially invalid economic protectionism.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner truckers' summary judgment motion as to a claim that a statute that imposed an axle tax violated the Commerce Clause and awarded their requested tax refund. The court found that the statute was facially invalid economic protectionism because simultaneously enacted registration fee reductions virtually insulated locally registered vehicles from the financial impact of the tax by almost always offsetting assessments.

 

876 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Quaker Valley School Dist.


No. 87 T.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


86 Pa. Commw. 496; 486 A.2d 526; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2122


November 15, 1984, Argued  
December 27, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied June 18, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Adrienne Brown v. Quaker Valley School District and Kenneth Johns, No. GD-83-1796.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured student sought review of an order from Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant school district and teacher. Plaintiff contended that governmental immunity was not available to defendant school district, that conduct of defendant teacher took him outside official immunity offered by statute, and raised constitutional challenges to immunity in tort actions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured student brought suit against defendant school district and teacher seeking to recover damages for injuries she suffered while in a physical education class. On appeal, the court affirmed the order from the trial court granting summary judgment in favor of defendants and dismissing plaintiff's complaint. The court found that the spring board and vaulting horse on which plaintiff was injured were items of moveable property and were not fixtures as they were not permanently placed at the school nor essential for its operation. That equipment was not a part of the real property, therefore, the care, custody, or control of real property exception to governmental immunity did not apply. The court held that defendant teacher was acting within the scope of his official duties at the time of the incident and was entitled to immunity under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 8545. The activity of the class, gymnastics, was an activity normally associated with an indoor physical education program. The court also found that governmental and official tort immunity was constitutional under the state and federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant school district and teacher. The gymnastics equipment on which plaintiff injured student was injured was not part of the real property and the care, custody, and control of real property exception to governmental immunity did not apply. Defendant teacher was acting within the scope of his duties at the time plaintiff was injured. Governmental tort immunity was constitutional.

Ins. Adjustment Bureau v. Ins. Comm'r for Pa.


No. 611 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


86 Pa. Commw. 491; 485 A.2d 858; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2115


September 10, 1984, Argued  
December 20, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of Insurance Adjustment Bureau v. The Insurance Commissioner for the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objection overruled. Respondent directed to file answer within thirty days.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Insurance Adjustment Bureau sought a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief against respondent Insurance Commissioner for the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, to have portions of 63 Pa .Cons. Stat. § 1605(a) declared unconstitutional, and to permanently enjoin respondent from enforcing those portions. Respondent objected, in the form of a demurrer, to petitioner's complaint.


OVERVIEW: In a complaint for declaratory relief, petitioner Insurance Adjustment Bureau alleged portions of 63 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1605(a) were unconstitutional as violative of its right to equal protection and to freedom of speech. Petitioner argued that private insurance companies, and other trades and businesses were not so limited and, therefore, petitioner's constitutional rights were violated. The court determined that petitioner Insurance Adjustment Bureau stated a viable cause of action for violation of its equal protection rights, and it appeared there was not an appropriate remedy at law to correct the asserted illegality.

In re Appeal of FPA Corp. from Decision of Newtown Township Zoning Hearing Bd.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania

Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources v. Fiore


No. 2083 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


88 Pa. Commw. 418; 491 A.2d 284; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2185


November 15, 1984, Argued  
December 7, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources v. William Fiore, d/b/a Municipal and Industrial Disposal Company, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary injunction denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources, applied for a preliminary injunction to enjoin respondent hazardous waste corporation from refusing access to its property to petitioner's employees who had sought to conduct inspections and collect effluent and ground water samples. The court's order that found respondent in civil contempt for having violated a consent agreement was pending appeal to the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: The court's order which found petitioner hazardous waste corporation in civil contempt for having violated a consent agreement with respondent, Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources, had been appealed by respondent corporation. In the meantime, petitioner applied for a preliminary injunction to enjoin respondent from refusing access to its property to petitioner's employees who sought to conduct inspections and collect effluent and ground water samples. The court held that the injunction application was ancillary to the initial contempt action that sought to determine whether respondent had complied with the court's order, and was therefore properly before the court. The court denied the injunction, and held that although Solid Waste Management Act § 608, 35 P.S. § 6018.608, and Clean Streams Law § 5(b)(8), 35 P.S. § 691.5(b)(8), were intended to allow petitioner to conduct warrantless searches, the grant of power was violative of U.S. Const. amend. IV. The statutes lacked most of the necessary qualities which would make them constitutionally sound, where they did not attempt to set up an inspection scheme which could arguably be a substitute for a warrant.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner, Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Resources, a preliminary injunction granting them access to petitioner hazardous waste corporation's property for inspection, because although the Pennsylvania Solid Waste Management Act and Clean Streams Law were intended to allow warrantless searches, such searches violated U.S. Const. amend. IV absent an inspection scheme that could arguably have substituted for a warrant.

El Concilio de Los Trabajadores de La Industria de Los Hongos v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Environmental Resources

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


86 Pa. Commw. 219; 484 A.2d 817; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2067


September 10, 1984, Argued  
November 30, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of El Concilio De Los Trabajadores De La Industria De Los Hongos by Trustee ad Litem Luis Guzman, Farm-workers Council of Berks County by Trustee ad Litem Christobal Rivera, Luis Guzman, Edilia Guzman, Wilfredo Negron, Jose Cruz, Luis A. OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed an action against respondent Department of Environmental Resources seeking a declaration that 25 Pa. Petitioners also sought relief in mandamus directing respondent to inspect all farm labor camp housing provided in the mushroom industry. The court denied petitioners' request for mandamus relief.

The court held that respondent Department of Environmental Resources had a duty to inspect farm labor camp housing provided in the mushroom industry to four or more unrelated persons and that the guidelines used by respondent to determine whether farm labor camp existed were invalid. The court denied petitioners' motion insofar as it requested mandamus relief.

Commonwealth ex rel. Roth v. Kozakiewicz


No. 82 T.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


86 Pa. Commw. 459; 485 A.2d 843; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2092


November 20, 1984, Heard  
November 21, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. Reverend D. Douglas Roth v. Charles J. Kozakiewicz, Warden of the Allegheny County Jail.



DISPOSITION: Rule discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and pastor both sought review in the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, in which the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County issued a preliminary injunction ordering petitioner to desist from performing pastoral functions at his Lutheran church and not to interfere with the performance of pastoral functions.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pastor was held in contempt and jailed after he was ordered to desist from performing any pastoral functions at the Trinity Evangelical Lutheran Church or any other church in that denomination, and not to interfere directly or indirectly with the performance of pastoral functions at that church by other pastors designated to perform them by the Bishop of the Synod. Petitioner appeared in the pulpit of that church on the following Sunday, and the pastor designated to appear by the Synod was excluded. Petitioner was committed to jail and filed a writ of habeas corpus after an adjudication of contempt. The court held the contempt order was civil where it imposed prison conditional on an expression of willingness to comply with the decree of the court. The court held that habeas corpus proceedings could be heard by the appellate court, if a court has ordered contempt in a civil matter, the appellate court would not dismiss the petition on procedural grounds but would consider the committing court's legal power to impose incarceration. The court held the trial judge followed proper procedure and allowed full due process.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the rule because the trial court followed proper procedure, allowed due process and proceeded by proper civil contempt to require petitioner to obey the injunction or be imprisoned. The punishment did not violate due process or exercise of free religion rights where petitioner was ordered not to go to a specific church within his denomination.

 

882 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Smithkline Beckman Corp. v. Commonwealth


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


85 Pa. Commw. 437; 482 A.2d 1344; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2191


Argued: June 5, 1984 - Philadelphia  
October 11, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 593 C.D. 1982


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation appealed the order of the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which sustained the resettlement of petitioner's capital stock tax for the year ending December 1977 by the Department of Revenue of respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The issue was whether dividends and interest on obligations of the United States should be included in the sales factor of 72 P.S. § 7401(3)2.(a)(15).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation disputed an order of the Board of Finance and Revenue, which sustained the resettlement of petitioner's 1977 capital stock tax by the Department of Revenue of respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The court held that 61 Pa. Code § 153.26(a), which mandated the exclusion of dividends and interest on United States obligations from the sales factor of the capital stock tax apportionment, controlled the case. The interpretation embodied in the regulation was reasonable and not clearly inconsistent with the statute. The court distinguished a case decided under 72 P.S. §§ 3420a - 3420-11. Because the status of dividends was disputable, the court applied rules of statutory construction to determine the legislative intent. The inclusion in the sales factor of dividends and interest on the obligations could result in the statute being held unconstitutional, so the court held that they were excluded. Further, respondent had presented its arguments as equally applicable to the dividends and to the interest. The court reversed, entered judgment for petitioner corporation, and remanded for a recomputation of the capital stock tax liability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, entered judgment for petitioner corporation, and remanded for a recomputation of the capital stock tax liability. The Department of Revenue's own regulation, which mandated the exclusion of dividends and interest on United States obligations from the sales factor, controlled the case. The interpretation embodied in the regulation was reasonable, not clearly inconsistent with the statute, and therefore not to be disregarded.

Fischer v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Public Welfare


No. 283 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


85 Pa. Commw. 240; 482 A.2d 1148; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1693


May 1, 1984, Argued  
September 20, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of JoAnne Fischer et al. v. Department of Public Welfare et al.



DISPOSITION: Exception sustained in part and overruled in part. Enforcement of reporting provisions of statutes enjoined. Judgment in favor of respondents with respect to remaining statutory provisions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both the commonwealth and petitioner citizens and others, filed exceptions to a decree by the chancellor declaring statutes which restricted the expenditure of public funds for abortion, 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 453 and the Abortion Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 321(c), unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner citizens and others sought declaratory and injunctive relief prohibiting the commonwealth from implementing statutes, which restricted the expenditure of public funds for abortions. The chancellor issued a decree, which declared the legislation unconstitutional and prohibited the enforcement of 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 453 and the 1982 Abortion Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 321(c). Both parties filed exceptions to the decree. The court held that the language of the statutes did not violate the equal protection provision of the state constitution. The court held that the right to abortion was a qualified one and that the state was not prohibited from regulating abortion for purposes of safeguarding health or for protecting potential life. The court held the commonwealth was free to assert its preference for childbirth over abortion by allocation of public funds. The court also held that the legislation did not involve a gender based classification under the equal rights amendment, Pa. Const. art. I, § 28. The court sustained petitioners' exceptions to the reporting provisions of the legislation on grounds that they were an unconstitutional invasion of privacy.


OUTCOME: The court overruled petitioner citizens' exceptions, sustained the commonwealth's exceptions to the reporting provisions of the legislation restricting abortion, and overruled the commonwealth's other exceptions. The court held that it was not unconstitutional for the state to prohibit the use of public funding for abortion; the reporting provisions were unconstitutional because they were so intrusive as to violate the right of privacy.

Phila. Bd. of Pensions & Ret. v. Bordley


Nos. 941 C.D. 1983, 942 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 645; 481 A.2d 690; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1680


June 4, 1984, Submitted on Briefs  
September 4, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in the case of Lila Bordley v. Board of Pensions and Retirement, No. 2557 July Term, 1981 and in the case of George Bordley, Jr. v. Board of Pensions and Retirement, No. 536 June Term, 1982.
Bordley v. Bd. of Pensions & Retirement, 9 Phila. 416, 1983 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 81 (Pa. C.P., 1983)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee surviving spouse pension benefits although she did not meet the requirements of Philadelphia, Pa., Municipal Pension Ordinance § 211.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellee surviving spouse sought to recover the deceased's pension. Appellant denied recovery stating that appellee was not a designated beneficiary and did not meet the durational requirement. The trial court reversed the judgment in part finding that appellee was entitled to recover because the requirements as applied to her violated the equal protection laws. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment. According to the court, where the classification did not discriminate against a suspect class or impinge upon a fundamental right, the level of equal protection analysis which a court must apply was the rational relationship test. The court held that under the rational relationship test, a court could not examine the application of a statute to individual members of a class as part of its equal protection analysis. The court stated that if the classification bore some rational relationship to a legitimate government end, then it was within the legislative power, and would be upheld. The court concluded that the trial court erred in its analysis. The court found that the requirements served a rational relationship to the legislative purpose which was to prevent deathbed marriages.


OUTCOME: Judgment granting appellee surviving spouse her deceased husband's pension was reversed because appellee was not a named beneficiary and did not meet the durational requirement. The court held that the statute as applied to appellee was constitutional as it served a rational basis to the legislative purpose.

Cerrone v. Milton School Dist.


No. 29 T.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 395; 479 A.2d 675; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1640


June 4, 1984, Argued  
August 9, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northumberland County in the case of Gino Cerrone, a minor, by his parents and natural guardians, Diane Cerrone and Rocco Cerrone and Diane Cerrone and Rocco Cerrone, in their own right, and Santina Cerrone v. Milton School District, Borough of Milton, Evangelical Community Hospital and Amador Calderon, M.D., No. CV 82-76.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, injured student and his parents, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northumberland County (Pennsylvania), which sustained preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer filed by appellee school district. The trial court had dismissed appellant's complaint against the school district on the grounds that it was barred by 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8541-8564.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, student and his parents, sought review of an order that dismissed their complaint against appellee school district under the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act (act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8541-8564. Appellants contended that the act was unconstitutional because the legislature could not immunize the commonwealth from suit and that the act violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed and held that the legislature had the authority to determine that certain bars to suit were needed for the operation of local government. The court further held that the act was a valid exercise of legislative authority specifically granted by the constitution. The court held that the act also did not violate the equal protection clause because the classifications involved in the act had a fair and substantial relationship to the purpose of the act; the classifications created a threshold for the recovery of certain damages, thus serving to define clearly the extent of the risk, and bore a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint of appellant, student and his parents, against appellee school district and held that the suit was barred by statute. The court held that the statute was constitutional because the legislature had the authority to immunize the commonwealth from suit and that the immunity bore a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental interest.

County of Fayette v. Commonwealth


Nos. 2481 C.D. 1981, 2385 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 260; 479 A.2d 1153; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1610


May 1, 1984, Argued  
July 31, 1984, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Orders of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review in cases of In re: Claim of Richard A. Hoch, No. B-199273, and Claim of Joan L. Snyder, No. B-221053.



DISPOSITION: Order in case No. 2481 C.D. 1981 is reversed. Order in case No. 2385 C.D. 1983 is affirmed. Benefits denied both applicants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought review of a decision of respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (Pennsylvania), which granted benefits to claimant one, court appointed employee. Claimant two, court appointed employee, sought review of respondent's order that denied her benefits because she voluntarily quit work without necessitous and compelling reason.


OVERVIEW: In one of two consolidated appeals, respondent Unemployment Compensation Board of Review granted benefits to claimant one, court-appointed employee. In the other, respondent denied claimant two, court-appointed employee, benefits because she voluntarily quit work without necessitous and compelling reason. On appeal, the first decision was reversed, and the second was affirmed. The court held that neither claimant was entitled to unemployment compensation. The provisions of the Ethics Law, 65 P.S. §§ 410 and 412. could not provide a legal foundation for good cause with respect to either claimant. Provisions of the Ethics Law were held invalid to the extent that they invaded upon the state Supreme Court's authority to administer the courts. Because the Supreme Court's power to supervise the judicial system was not subject to legislative regulation, Ethics Law provisions that infringed upon that power were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that awarded claimant one unemployment benefits, and affirmed the decision denying claimant two unemployment benefits, holding that neither claimant was entitled to unemployment compensation. The court ruled that the provisions of the Ethics Law could not provide a legal foundation for good cause with respect to either claimant.

John Doe Corp. a v. Commonwealth


Nos. 3178 C.D. 1983, 3470 C.D. 1983, 3471 C.D. 1983, 3472 C.D. 1983, 3473 C.D. 1983, 3474 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 234; 479 A.2d 655; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1606


April 3, 1984, Argued  
July 30, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in case of John Doe Corporations A, B, C, D and E v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Pennsylvania Crime Commission, In Re: Petition for Enforcement of a Subpoena to John Doe Corp. "A", "B", "C", "D" and "E".



DISPOSITION: Petitions of corporations overruled. Corporations ordered to file answers to petitions of commission. Preliminary objections of commission sustained. Petition for review of commission is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporations filed preliminary objections to the subpoenas served upon them by respondent Pennsylvania Crime Commission to produce various documents. Respondent filed preliminary objections that raised a lack of jurisdiction and failure of the petition to conform to law or rule of court.


OVERVIEW: In an investigation pursuant to a resolution of respondent Pennsylvania Crime Commission, respondent served petitioner corporations subpoenas to produce various documents. Petitioners refused to comply, and filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer and a motion for a more specific pleading for review regarding respondent's subpoenas. Respondent filed preliminary objections that raised a lack of jurisdiction and the failure of the petition to conform to law or rule of court. The court held that respondent alleged sufficient facts that were proof against petitioners' preliminary objection. Respondent had the power to issue subpoenas pursuant to the Pennsylvania Crime Commission Act, 71 P.S. § 1190.7 independently of the enforcement procedure found in 71 P.S. § 1190.4(7). Further, pursuant to Administrative Code, § 520, 71 P.S. § 200, respondent was required to seek a court hearing on the merits of the subpoenas through a petition for enforcement. Finally, the subpoenas were not a misrepresentation of petitioners' rights or an abuse of respondent's power; they merely warned that petitioners' failure to comply could result in further action being taken.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner corporations request to dismiss the subpoenas issued by respondent Pennsylvania Crime Commission, holding respondent alleged sufficient facts, had the power to issue subpoenas, and was required to seek a hearing on the merits of the subpoenas. The court sustained respondent's preliminary objection, holding petitioners would have the opportunity to contest the subpoenas' validity during the enforcement proceeding.

 

888 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Philadelphia v. Cohen


No. 1206 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 200; 479 A.2d 32; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1588


June 4, 1984, Argued  
July 25, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in the case of City of Philadelphia v. Rami Cohen and Rami Electronics and Stereo Sound, Inc., No. 2003 September Term, 1982.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found specific provisions of its Noise and Excessive Vibration ordinance and regulations facially unconstitutional for vagueness and overbreadth. Plaintiff brought the action to restrain defendants, stereo electronics shop owners, from playing amplified, recorded music in violation of the ordinance and regulations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city cited defendant stereo electronics shop owners for emitting recorded, amplified music from their store at decibel levels violative of plaintiff's Noise and Excessive Vibration regulations. A determination was made that provisions of plaintiff's ordinance were facially unconstitutional for vagueness and overbreadth. On appeal, the decision was reversed. The court held that the regulation was not substantially overbroad. Whatever overbreadth existed was to be corrected through case-by-case analyses of specific factual situations, and not by generally invalidating the regulation for facial overbreadth. In addition, although the phrase "adverse psychological and physiological effects" lacked quantitative precision, the language embodied a normative standard sufficient to guide prospective conduct. Further, employment of a decibel scale to objectively measure amplified sound levels was not a denial of fair warning of prohibited conduct. The phrase "beyond the property boundary" was not unconstitutionally vague because the property boundary definition insured fair enforcement by designating the sound level measurement zone as that area beyond the property boundary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that plaintiff city's ordinance and regulations were facially unconstitutional because of vagueness and overbreadth, holding that when business enterprises were regulated, vagueness standards were relaxed. The court ruled that whatever overbreadth existed was to be corrected through case-by-case analyses of specific factual situations, and not by generally invalidating the regulation for facial overbreadth.

 

889 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Knudsen v. Delaware County Regional Water Quality Control Authority


Nos. 29 T.D. 1983, 30 T.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


84 Pa. Commw. 36; 478 A.2d 533; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1581


June 4, 1984, Argued  
July 18, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County in cases of Kathleen Knudsen and Richard W. Knudsen, as parents and natural guardians of Susan Knudsen, a minor, and Kathleen Knudsen, in her own right v. Delaware County Regional Water Quality Control Authority and Percy Jones, The Borough of Upland, Chester-Upland School District, No. 82-9246, and in the case of Kathleen Knudsen, Administratrix of the Estate of David E. Knudsen, Deceased v. Delaware County Regional Water Quality Control Authority and Percy Jones, Borough of Upland and Chester-Upland School District, No. 82-9247.



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed in part and modified in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, administratrix of the estate of her deceased son, challenged a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, Delaware County (Pennsylvania), in actions for negligence and wrongful death, where the trial court had sustained the preliminary objection of appellee Borough of Upland, Chester-Upland School District.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the administratrix of the estate of her deceased son, brought two complaints; one for the wrongful death of her son, who had been killed when struck by a truck while crossing the street near his school during luncheon recess, and one for her emotional injury, allegedly sustained when appellant saw her dying son. Appellant sued the driver of the truck and his employer, and joined appellee Borough of Upland, Chester-Upland School District (school district) in a third-party complaint, alleging that it had failed to notify the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation and/or the Borough of Upland regarding a non-operative speed limit sign. The trial court sustained the preliminary objection of appellee school district in the nature of a demurrer, and dismissed the third-party complaint, holding that the legislature constitutionally conferred immunity upon school districts. 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8541, 8542. Appellants contended that the statute was unconstitutional. The court affirmed as to sustaining the preliminary objections. Without sufficient evidence, the court modified the trial court's judgment to permit appellant leave to join appellee school district as a defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the trial court sustaining the preliminary objection of appellee Borough of Upland, Chester-Upland School District (school district), in appellant's actions for negligence and wrongful death, because the statute conferring immunity upon school districts was constitutional. The court modified the order of the trial court to permit appellant leave to add appellee school district as a defendant.

 

890 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Temple University-of Commonwealth System of Higher Education v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Public Welfare


Nos. 770 C.D. 1982, 1111 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


83 Pa. Commw. 516; 480 A.2d 1267; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1543


April 2, 1984, Argued  
July 6, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Department of Public Welfare in case of Re: Appeal of Temple University Hospital of Audit Disallowances relating to the Cost Reporting Periods July 1, 1974 to June 30, 1977, dated April 13, 1982.



DISPOSITION: Decision regarding reimbursement of interest expenses vacated and remanded. Decision regarding reimbursement for physician services affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner university sought review of respondent public welfare department order disallowing reimbursement costs under Pennsylvania's medical assistance program, Public Welfare Code, 62 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 443.1.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner university sought review of respondent welfare department order disallowing reimbursement under Pennsylvania's medical assistance program, Public Welfare Code, 62 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 443.1, contending the denial improperly disallowed hospital operational interest expenses and applied an improper reimbursement formula. The court affirmed the order to the extent it applied to reimbursement for medical school physician rendered services based on the conclusion the proper formula was applied. The court explained 42 C.F.R. § 405.427 governed such reimbursement regardless of whether the services and facilities were supplied by a teaching hospital or not. The court vacated and remanded for further proceedings with regard to interest expenses. The court concluded petitioner was prejudiced by a procedural irregularity that denied the benefit of favorable findings from a reopened and consolidated appeal when such findings would otherwise have been binding on respondent. The court explained the hearing examiner also failed to bifurcate his analysis by years as required while his fiscal year allocation was affected by what was considered an unverified preceding period balance.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed respondent welfare department's denial of physician service reimbursement costs because proper formula was applied to petitioner teaching hospital. Court vacated and remanded denial of interest expenses in petitioner hospital's favor because prejudice was sustained by procedural irregularity where benefit of favorable factual findings which otherwise would have been binding was denied based on appeal reopening and consolidation.

 

891 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Fraenzl v. Secretary of Commonwealth


No. 1247 C.D. 1984


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


83 Pa. Commw. 539; 478 A.2d 903; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1601


May 29, 1983, Heard  
June 8, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Clare M. Fraenzl and The Socialist Workers Party v. The Secretary of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and the Pennsylvania Bureau of Legislation, Commissions and Elections.



DISPOSITION: Application to intervene denied. Petition for reconsideration and/or rehearing filed by intervenor. Petition denied. Motion of petitioner for peremptory judgment granted. Nomination papers to be accepted and name to be placed on ballot for November 1984 election.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a candidate and the Socialist Workers Party, sought a peremptory judgment in mandamus compelling respondent Secretary of the Commonwealth and the Pennsylvania Bureau of Legislation, Commissions, and Elections to accept petitioner candidate's nomination papers and place her on the general election ballot as candidate for the office of the United States Representative for the 22nd Congressional District.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, a candidate and the Socialist Workers Party (SWP), sought a peremptory judgment in mandamus compelling respondent Secretary of the Commonwealth and the Pennsylvania Bureau of Legislation, Commissions, and Elections to accept petitioner's nomination papers and place her on the general election ballot as candidate for the office of the United States Representative for the 22nd Congressional District. There were only three registered SWP voters in the district. Petitioner submitted her nomination papers with the requisite number of signatures under 25 P.S. § 2911 and her papers were rejected under the Election Code §§ 902 and 976, 25 P.S. §§ 2862 and 2936. The court granted the motion for peremptory judgment, holding that 25 P.S. §§ 2862 and 2936 did not impose any duty on a political group to actively register voters in the party name once party status was achieved under 25 P.S. § 2831(b). The legislature intended to make the nomination of candidates a significantly easier task once a political group had demonstrated its viability through the performance of its candidates in a general election.


OUTCOME: The motion by petitioners, a candidate and the Socialist Workers Party, for peremptory judgment was granted and petitioner candidate's nomination papers were accepted and her name placed on the ballot, because petitioners did not have a duty to actively register voters in the party name once its party status was achieved.

 

892 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Condemnation of Land


No. 3043 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


83 Pa. Commw. 76; 476 A.2d 489; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1563


April 4, 1984, Argued  
June 5, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County in the case of In Re: Condemnation of land in Township of Damascus, Wayne County, Pennsylvania, D.B. 187 -- Page 3, Property of Lloyd E. Canfield and Eloise L. Canfield, his wife, No. 2, E.D. 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Wayne County (Pennsylvania), which granted the preliminary objections of appellees, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and the Department of Environmental Resources, and dismissed appellants' action for damages to the use of their property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners brought an action against appellees, the state and Department of Environmental Resources, alleging that appellees unlawfully ceded appellants' riparian rights, damaging the use of their property. Appellees responded with preliminary objections raising the statute of limitations. The court of common pleas granted appellees' preliminary objections and dismissed appellants' action, and appellants sought review. The court affirmed, as the applicable statutes of limitations barred appellants' claims. The court found that appellants' claims accrued in either 1956 or 1967, and they filed their action in 1982. The court concluded that, even using 1967, the two-year statute of limitations for a continuing trespass claims had run, as had the six-year statute of limitations for eminent domain claims. The court found that appellants' claim that the harm to their property constituted a taking and was subject to a 21 year statute of limitations failed, as the cause of action was fully barred because the statute of limitations had run prior to the effective date of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5530(a)(3).


OUTCOME: The order of the court of common pleas that granted the preliminary objections of appellees, the state and the Department of Environmental Resources, and dismissed appellant landowners' action for damages to the use of their property was affirmed, as all of appellants' causes of action were fully barred by the applicable statutes of limitations.

 

893 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Leonard v. Thornburgh


No. 1724 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


83 Pa. Commw. 1; 477 A.2d 577; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1482


March 13, 1984, Argued  
May 31, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of Thomas A. Leonard and Kathleen Leonard and Graveley Roofing Corporation v. Richard L. Thornburgh, Governor, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and James I. Scheiner, Secretary of Revenue, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and William J. Green, Mayor, City of Philadelphia, and Joseph W. Brown, Director of Finance, City of Philadelphia and Eugene L. Cliett, Jr., Revenue Commissioner, City of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Motion of petitioner granted in part and denied in part. Motion of defendant denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review in the court's original equity jurisdiction challenging the constitutionality of respondent city's non-resident wage tax cap and related ordinance. The parties made cross motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city enacted an ordinance that amended its wage and net profits tax by increasing the tax rate for all taxpayers for residents of respondent city only. The rate for all non-resident taxpayers remained the same. Petitioner sought review in the court's original equity jurisdiction challenging the constitutionality of the non-resident wage tax cap and the ordinance. On cross motions for summary judgment, the court granted petitioner's motion for summary judgment in part. Under the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, the court declared unconstitutional the non-resident wage tax cap and ordinance, which were enacted pursuant to 72 P.S. § 7359(b), the so-called Philadelphia Non-Resident Wage Tax Cap. The court said that the Pennsylvania Constitution clearly required uniformity within the territorial limits of an authority levying a tax.


OUTCOME: The court granted in part petitioner's motion for summary judgment. The court declared unconstitutional the so-called Philadelphia Non-Resident Wage Tax Cap and the ordinance so amending respondent city's wage and net profits tax, because the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, required uniformity within the territorial limits of an authority levying a tax. The holding was prospective, to take effect on July 1, 1984.

 

894 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Boyle Land & Fuel Co. v. Commonwealth, Environmental Hearing Bd.


No. 1989 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


82 Pa. Commw. 452; 475 A.2d 928; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1432


March 13, 1984, Argued  
May 15, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in the case of Boyle Land and Fuel Company v. The Environmental Hearing Board; Edward Gerjuoy, Member; Anthony J. Mazullo, Jr., Member, and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources.



DISPOSITION: Respondent's motion granted. Petitioner's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner land and fuel company commenced this action in the court's original jurisdiction (Pennsylvania) seeking equitable and declaratory judgment relief. Petitioner's sought to declare Section 18.4 of the Surface Mining Conservation and Reclamation Act, 52 P.S. § 1396.22, and Section 605 of the Clean Streams Law, 35 P.S. § 691.605, unconstitutional. The Commonwealth filed a cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the court was whether either party was entitled to summary judgment. There appeared to be no factual dispute. Petitioner land and fuel company contended that the bond requirements of the Surface Mining Conservation and Reclamation Act, 52 P.S. § 1396.22, and the Clean Streams Law, 35 P.S. § 691.605, violated the mandatory right of appeal created by Pa. Const. art. V, § 9. The court held that the bond requirement was a reasonable condition on petitioner's land and fuel company's right to appeal an assessment. The court held that the bond requirement ensured the underlying validity of appeals and served to protect the public interest in a safe and clean environment. The court held that a party challenging the constitutionality of a statutory provision had a heavy burden to overcome the presumption of its constitutionality. The court concluded that petitioner did not meet that burden. The court held that summary judgment could be granted when there was no dispute as to any material issue of fact. The court held that since there were no material facts in dispute, the Commonwealth was entitled to summary judgment as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner land and fuel company's motion for summary judgment and granted the Commonwealth's cross motion for summary judgment. The court held that a party challenging the constitutionality of a statute had a heavy burden to overcome the presumption of its constitutionality, and petitioner did not meet that burden.

 

895 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Fridenberg v. Trustees of University of Pennsylvania


No. 3251 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


82 Pa. Commw. 100; 474 A.2d 411; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1378


April 4, 1983, Argued  
April 26, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by Estate of Fridenberg v. Trustees of University of Pennsylvania, 506 Pa. 569, 487 A.2d 345, 1985 Pa. LEXIS 285 (1985)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Orphans' Court Division, in the case of Estate of Mone S. Fridenberg, No. 3804 of 1931.
Estate of Fridenberg, 5 Phila. 127, 1980 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 107 (Pa. C.P., 1980)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant university challenged a decision from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which held that a charitable gift made to it by testator had failed under 20 P.S. § 195 of the Wills Act of 1917. Appellant contended that § 195 had been invalidated and that it should no longer be precluded from recovering under the terms of the will.


OVERVIEW: The residuary clause of a will provided that a trust would be created for the benefit of a several individuals, including the decedent's wife and sister. However, upon their deaths, a sum was to be paid from the trust principle to appellant university. Shortly thereafter the account of the personal representative was audited. The auditing judge noted that under the Wills Act of 1917, 20 P.S. § 195, the charitable trust could not be established since the testator died within 30 days of executing the will. Years later, after the death of testator's wife and sister it was adjudicated that appellant's gift had failed. Approximately 25 years later, § 195 was declared unconstitutional and of no effect. When the last of the beneficiaries of the trust died the estates of the wife and sister appellant claimed the remainder. The auditing judge awarded the funds to the estates, based on the adjudication that the bequest to appellant had failed. On appeal, the court held that the undistributed portions of the trust were not subject to the prior audits or adjudications and that, due to the invalidation of § 195, the remainder of the trust must go to appellant.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court, to award the remainder of a trust to the estates of the testator's wife and sister rather than appellant university, was reversed. The court held that the statute invalidating the gift to appellant had been declared unconstitutional and that the portion of the trust, which the will originally designated as a gift to appellant, should be awarded to appellant in accordance with the provisions of the will.

Chambers Dev. Co. v. Commonwealth


No. 499 C.D. 1983, No. 500 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


81 Pa. Commw. 622; 474 A.2d 728; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1370


March 12, 1984, Argued  
April 23, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. Allegheny County Health Department v. Chambers Development Company, Inc., No. S.A. 1478 of 1981 and No. S.A. 255 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Decision of District Justice affirmed. Appeal regarding decision of hearing officer quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company sought review of the decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which found that appellant violated sewage sludge regulations.


OVERVIEW: Appellant company was found guilty of summary violations for improperly accepting sewage sludge at its landfill. Appellant contended that appellee county health department had no enforcement authority at its landfill, and appellant alleged that an agreement between appellant and the Department of Environmental Resources (DER) provided that the DER was to be the sole enforcement agency. The court agreed with the trial court that the agreement did not prevent other governmental units from enforcing any environmental law that it was empowered to enforce. Additionally, appellee was not a party to the DER contract and was not bound. The court agreed that the DER took precedence when a conflict existed between its administration and that of appellee, but found it irrelevant as the DER assured appellee that its activities did not conflict with those of the DER. Finally, 35 P.S. § 6018.106 did not unconstitutionally delegate authority to appellee as the statute contained adequate standards for guidance. The court affirmed the trial court's finding that appellant committed sewage sludge violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that appellant company committed sewage sludge violations. The court found that appellee county health department was permitted to enforce regulations prohibiting sewage sludge acceptance, and appellee's authority was not preempted by an agreement between appellant and the Department of Environmental Resources.

Consumer Party of Pennsylvania v. Commonwealth


No. 2841 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


81 Pa. Commw. 609; 475 A.2d 850; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1353


December 6, 1983, Argued  
April 19, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in the case of Consumer Party of Pennsylvania et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al.



DISPOSITION: Motion of plaintiff denied; motion of State Treasurer and the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania granted. Preliminary objections sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff consumer party challenged the constitutionality of that part of 65 P.S. § 366.1, which provided for increases in pay and expenses for the members of the general assembly. Plaintiff and defendants, treasurer and commonwealth, filed cross-motions for summary judgment. Defendant legislators filed objections, contending that the action was barred by the Speech and Debate Clause, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15.


OVERVIEW: In an action for declaratory and injunctive relief, plaintiff consumer party challenged the constitutionality of 65 P.S. § 366.1, which increased salaries and expenses for defendant legislators. Plaintiff and defendants treasurer and commonwealth filed cross-motions for summary judgment and defendant legislators filed objections contending that the Speech and Debate Clause, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15, barred the action against them. The court agreed, sustaining defendant legislators' objections and granting summary judgment for defendants, treasurer and commonwealth. Because the clause protected all activities within the legitimate legislative sphere, the court dismissed the action against defendant legislators. Denying plaintiff's motion for summary judgment, the court found that 65 P.S. § 366.1 did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 27 because it did not increase the salaries of any current members of the general assembly and because the expense allowance was not unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court sustained defendant legislator's objections and granted summary judgment for defendants, treasurer and commonwealth, in the action of plaintiff consumer party. The state constitution's speech and debate clause barred the action against defendant legislators. Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment was denied because the challenged statute did not increase current defendant legislator's salaries and the increases were reasonable.

Yeckley v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp.


No. 3002 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


81 Pa. Commw. 576; 474 A.2d 71; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1345


January 30, 1983, Submitted on Briefs  
April 13, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied July 2, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Thomas P. Yeckley, Sr., Nos. SA 503 and 553 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed his consolidated appeals from the department of transportation's revocation of his driving privileges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of driving under the influence of alcohol or a controlled substance, in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731, on four separate occasions. Pursuant to the "habitual offender" provision, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1542, the department of transportation revoked appellant's driving privileges. Appellant's consolidated appeals from the department of transportation's action was dismissed by the trial court. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's order. The court rejected appellant's constitutional challenges against § 1542. The court determined that the notice and opportunity to be heard requirements of due process were met by virtue of the de novo hearing in the trial court. The court found that habitual offenders were not a suspect class and that driving was not a fundamental right. Therefore, the court determined that § 1542 clearly passed constitutional muster under a rational relation standard. The court rejected appellant's contention that revocation was a cruel and unusual punishment because such a revocation, after appellant had been convicted of drunk driving on four separate occasions, did not shock the general conscience.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

In re Appeal of deBotton


No. 2368 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


81 Pa. Commw. 513; 474 A.2d 706; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1338


December 7, 1983, Argued  
April 12, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County in case of In Re: Appeal of Claude deBotton from the denial by the Zoning Hearing Board of Springfield Township of his Application for Variance and/or Special Exception from the present zoning classification to use premises at Church Lane and Baltimore Pike, Springfield, Delaware County, Pennsylvania, for commercial stores and offices, Nos. 81-02416 and 81-10484.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Order of board reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which ordered appellant's zoning board to grant a "validity" zoning variance. Appellee landowner had claimed that appellant township's zoning ordinances had rendered his property valueless, which entitled him to a validity variance, and that the ordinance enforced by appellant township was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellee landowner was denied a variance to develop a nine acre tract which had been zoned for office space. Appellant township's zoning board had determined that no special exceptions were provided for by the statute, and that appellee landowner did not qualify for a "validity" variance. The trial court determined that the variance should have been granted and ordered appellant township's zoning board to grant the variance. On appeal, the court reversed because it determined that the trial court had exceeded its scope of review in considering a zoning decision, and that it improperly reassessed the credibility of witnesses. The court held that the trial court's proper scope of review was limited to a determination of abuse of discretion, constitutionality, and whether substantial evidence existed in the record to support the decision of the board. The court found that appellee had not demonstrated that the zoning ordinance had rendered his property valueless, or that it had created invalid spot zoning because surrounding pieces of property within the same designation had been successfully developed as restricted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that required the appellant township's zoning board to grant a variance to appellee landowner because the trial court had exceeded its scope of review when it considered the evidence and passed on the credibility of witnesses, and appellee had failed to prove that his property was rendered valueless by the ordinance, or that the ordinance constituted prohibited spot zoning..

 

900 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Sunny Farms, Ltd. v. North Codorus Township


No. 308 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


81 Pa. Commw. 371; 474 A.2d 56; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1318


May 11, 1983, Argued  
April 6, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County in the case of North Codorus Township v. Sunny Farms, Ltd., No. 80-C-3000.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged the order of the Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which prohibited the construction and operation of an underground, hazardous waste disposal facility in violation of North Codorus, Pa., Zoning Ordinance No. 18, § 4(c). The ordinance prohibited operation of a sanitary landfill within 500 yards of a structure occupied by humans.


OVERVIEW: Appellant company sought to construct and operate an underground, hazardous waste refuse facility. Appellee township was granted a preliminary injunction against the facility because it violated North Codorus, Pa., Zoning Ordinance No. 18, § 4(c), which prohibited the facility from being within 500 yards of a structure occupied by humans. Appellant challenged the validity of the zoning ordinance on constitutional and state preemption grounds. The court affirmed the order of the lower court and found that while appellee could not set geological or engineering standards stricter than those established by the Department of Environmental Resources, under 35 P.S. § 6018.105(h), it was not prohibited from enacting restrictions relating to aesthetics, population density, and accessibility. The court found that appellee had a substantial interest in protecting and promoting the public health, property values and aesthetics of the community, and that appellant had failed to show that the 500 yard zoning restriction was not substantially related to the public health, safety, and welfare of the community.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that prohibited appellant company from constructing and operating an hazardous waste disposal facility in violation of appellee township's zoning ordinance because appellee had a substantial interest in protecting the health, property values, and aesthetics of its community, and it was not preempted from enacting zoning restrictions related to those issues, even where a state certificate of need had been issued.

 

901 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Fischer v. Dep't of Public Welfare


No. 283 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


85 Pa. Commw. 215; 482 A.2d 1137; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1699


February 7, 1984, Heard  
March 9, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original Jurisdiction in case of Joanne Fischer et al. v. Department of Public Welfare et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree Nisi issued. Respondents permanently enjoined from enforcing statutory provisions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged the constitutionality of legislation prohibiting the use of commonwealth funds or federal funds appropriated by the commonwealth to fund abortions unless either a physician certified that the life of the mother would be endangered if the fetus were carried to full term or the pregnancy resulted from rape or incest, which was promptly reported.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners claimed statutes providing that no commonwealth funds or federal funds appropriated by the commonwealth be used to fund abortions, unless a physician certified that the mother's life would be endangered if the fetus were carried to full term or the pregnancy resulted from promptly reported rape or incest. The court permanently enjoined enforcement of the provisions of 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 453 and 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3215(c), finding that § 453 offended the equal protection provisions of the Pennsylvania Constitution and violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, and that § 3215(c) offended the equal protection provisions of the Pennsylvania Constitution, violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, and violated the right to privacy guaranteed to victims of rape and incest by the state and federal constitutions. The court found that the funding restriction unduly burdened the fundamental right of indigent women to have medically necessary abortions, that the statutes made a discriminatory sex-based distinction, and that the state's interest in prompt reporting of rape and incest was not so compelling as to override the obvious and injurious invasion of a victim's right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court enjoined enforcement of statutes prohibiting use of commonwealth funds or federal funds appropriated by the commonwealth for abortions, except if a physician certified that the mothers' life would be endangered if the fetus were carried to term or if a pregnancy resulted from promptly reported rape or incest, holding that the statutes violated rights to equal protection and privacy, and the Pennsylvania Equal Rights Amendment.

American Federation of State, etc. v. Commonwealth


Nos. 2064 C.D. 1983, 2133 C.D. 1983, 2160 C.D. 1983 and 2172 C.D. 1983


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


80 Pa. Commw. 611; 472 A.2d 746; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1263; 5 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 1313


November 15, 1983, Argued  
March 7, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in cases of American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, AFL-CIO etc. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al.; Joint Bargaining Committee of the Pennsylvania Social Services Union et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al.; Dennis J. Solecki et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al., and The Association of Pennsylvania State College and University Faculties et al. v. The State System of Higher Education et al.



DISPOSITION: Petitioners' motion for summary judgment granted. Respondents' motion for summary judgment denied. Implementation of statutory amendment enjoined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees and respondent commonwealth filed cross-motions for summary judgment, and petitioners sought permanent enjoinment of 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5505.1.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employees and respondent commonwealth filed cross-motions for summary judgment. Petitioners sought the permanent enjoinment of 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5505.1, which required most commonwealth employees, participants in the State Employees' Retirement System, to contribute an additional one and one-quarter percent of their salary to the State Employees' Retirement Fund. Respondents sought to have the constitutionality of § 5505.1 upheld. The court granted petitioners' motion for summary judgment and denied respondents' motion for summary judgment. The court ordered respondent to refund immediately all employee contributions in excess of the five percent basic contribution rate previously collected. The court held that, as to the non-vested petitioners, § 5505.1 was an unconstitutional impairment of the right to contract.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner employees' motion for summary judgment and denied respondent commonwealths' motion because a statute requiring petitioners to contribute additional money to the State Employment Retirement System was an unconstitutional impairment of the right to contract.

Tuckfelt v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


No. 881 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


80 Pa. Commw. 496; 471 A.2d 1311; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1240


October 3, 1983, Argued  
February 29, 1984, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Melvin Tuckfelt and Freda Tuckfelt, husband and wife v. Zoning Board of Adjustment of the City of Pittsburgh, No. SA 1274 of 1979.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant building owners challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a denial by appellee city zoning board of adjustment of their request for either a special exception or an occupancy permit to allow two roomers to lawfully occupy the third floor of appellants' building.


OVERVIEW: Appellant building owners rented rooms on the third floor of their building for years in violation of the city zoning ordinance. Appellants challenged the lower court order which affirmed appellee city zoning board of adjustment's denial of their request for either a special exception or an occupancy permit. On appeal, the court affirmed, and held that 53 P.S. § 25057, permitting appellee to render decisions within a reasonable time, was satisfied, where the five-month delay from the hearing until the decision was not unreasonable; appellants continued to rent the rooms, and therefore suffered no prejudice. Testimony of increased traffic, no available parking, litter, loud noises, and unseemly conduct constituted substantial evidence sufficient to support the finding that increased occupancy of the subject property in the R-1A highest class residential district was detrimental to the public health, morals, safety, and general welfare of the neighborhood, and the finding was legally sufficient to support a denial of a special exception request. The court stated that mere delay in enforcement did not create a vested right to use property in violation of zoning regulations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed denial of a special exception or occupancy permit, and held that the five-month delay from the hearing to appellee city zoning board of adjustment's decision was not unreasonable; testimony of no available parking, litter, loud noises, etc., was sufficient to support a denial of a special exception request, and mere delay in enforcement did not create a vested right to use property in violation of zoning regulations.

 

904 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Windrick v. Commonwealth


No. 2473 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


80 Pa. Commw. 401; 471 A.2d 924; 1984 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1221


October 5, 1983, Submitted on Briefs  
February 23, 1984, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied August 6, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County in the case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Milo Windrick, No. 1562 of 1982.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which determined that it was without jurisdiction to entertain a petition to appeal nunc pro tunc the suspension of appellant's driving privileges by appellee state driver's licensing bureau.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver challenged the order of the trial court, which determined that it was without jurisdiction to entertain a petition to appeal the suspension of driving privileges by appellee state driver's licensing bureau. Appellant had filed his petition more than 30 days after the date of mailing of the official notice of suspension of his driver's license, contrary to the requirement of 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5571(b), 5572. Appellant contended that the appeal period should have been measured from the time of receipt of the notice of suspension and not the time of mailing, and that the provisions of the statutes deprived him of his constitutional rights of due process and equal protection. The court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that the statute did not present a clear, palpable, or plain violation of the constitution. All prospective appellants were subject to the delivery offered by the postal service, and the likely number of days for mail delivery were deemed to create a reasonable appeal period. Because appellant's filing of the petition was not timely, the trial court did not have the requisite subject matter jurisdiction to entertain it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order, which declined jurisdiction over appellant driver's petition to appeal the suspension of driving privileges imposed by appellee state driver's licensing bureau, because of untimely filing of the petition. The court held that the statutes, which measured the 30-day appeal period from the date of mailing, were not unconstitutional.
