Samuel J. Lansberry, Inc. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 192 C.D. 1981, No. 226 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


66 Pa. Commw. 381; 444 A.2d 832; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1263


December 18, 1981, Argued  
May 3, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission in case of Paul E. Davidson, Mahlon E. Davidson and Harold Davidson, Jr., t/a Davidson Brothers -- Amendment to permit transportation of coal from points in the counties of Centre, Clearfield and Clinton, to points in Pennsylvania, Docket No. A.80975, Folder 1, Am-H.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner coal carrier sought review of the order of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, which granted intervenor coal transportation company a certificate of public convenience to transport coal through two specific counties.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner coal carrier challenged the order of respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission that granted intervenor coal transportation company a certificate of public convenience to transport coal through two specific counties. The order reversed the Administrative Law Judge's prior denial of the certificate. Petitioner argued that respondent violated the provisions of § 332(h) of the Public Utility Code, 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 332(h), in entertaining the exceptions and adopting its order. The court held that any error by respondent in entertaining the exceptions was a harmless one. The court reversed the portion of the order granting the certificate, holding that intervenor failed to establish that the existing services covering the areas concerned were inadequate. 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1103(a) provided that a certificate of public convenience would be granted only if respondent found that the granting of such certificate was necessary or proper for the service, accommodation, convenience, or safety of the public. The court held that intervenor was required to prove that petitioner's existing services were inadequate, and it failed to do so.


OUTCOME: The order granting intervenor coal transportation company a certificate of public convenience was reversed because intervenor was required to prove that petitioner coal carrier's existing services were inadequate, and intervenor failed to do so.

 

969 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Cavanaugh


No. 530 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


65 Pa. Commw. 620; 444 A.2d 1308; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1237



April 22, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reported at 65 Pa. Commw. 620 at 632.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Opinion of April 6, 1982, Reported at 65 Pa. Commw. 620.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Objections were filed to nomination petitions of a candidate for the office of justice of the Pennsylvania Supreme Court for alleged violations of § 912(b) of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2872(b).


OVERVIEW: Objections were filed to nomination petitions of a candidate for the office of justice of the Pennsylvania Supreme Court for alleged violations of § 912(b) of the Election Code, 25 P.S. § 2872(b). It was alleged that the candidate failed to obtain 100 valid signatures from each of five counties as required by § 2872(b). The candidate argued that the geographic distribution requirement was unconstitutional and that the nomination petition should be upheld. The court upheld the objections to the petition and held that the candidate failed to establish that § 2872(b) was unconstitutional. The court held that the minimal signature requirement imposed, combined with the cross-filing or independent access available to candidates, had no real and appreciable impact on the franchise rights of voters. The court held that the state's interests of ensuring serious candidates, ensuring manageable ballot size, and ensuring that prospective candidates had a significant modicum of support provided a rational basis for § 2872(b). Further, judges were not representatives, and the state's method of electing its judiciary was not subject to an equal protection challenge.


OUTCOME: The objections to the candidate's petitions were upheld because the candidate failed to prove that the geographic distribution requirement of the Election Code violated the candidate's equal protection rights.

 

970 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


West Penn Power Co. v. Cohen


No. 1339 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


66 Pa. Commw. 263; 443 A.2d 1366; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1814


December 15, 1981, Argued  
April 22, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of West Penn Power Co. v. Howard Cohen, Secretary, Pennsylvania Department of Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Mandamus denied. Declaratory judgment granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff power company filed a petition for a writ of mandamus with the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, seeking to compel defendant Secretary of Revenue to make settlement of tax returns filed under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 P.S. § 3171 and for declaratory relief as to the duties and responsibilities of the Secretary of Revenue.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff power company sought a writ of mandamus in order to compel defendant Secretary of Revenue to make settlement of tax returns filed under the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 P.S. § 3171. Defendant also sought declaratory relief from the court as to the duties and responsibilities of defendant as applied to the administration under 72 P.S. § 1. Defendant did not have a formal settlement procedure in place; therefore, taxpaying utilities were foreclosed from filing petitions for resettlement and were relegated to contesting overpayments under the refund provisions of 72 P.S. § 1. Based on a decision by the Attorney General, which imposed a duty on defendant to engage in settlement of taxes, plaintiff requested a mandamus, asserting that this opinion created a mandatory duty for defendant to settle these returns. The court held, as a matter of law, the provisions of 72 P.S. § 1 did not require that all 72 P.S. § 3171 returns needed to be settled by defendant. Accordingly, the court denied plaintiff's request for mandamus, but granted plaintiff's request for declaratory judgment.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's request for a writ of mandamus and granted its request for declaratory judgment because the court found that as a matter of law, Public Utility Tax returns did not need to be routinely settled under the provisions of the Fiscal Code; however, if the Department of Revenue intended to use the collection provisions of the Code, then it was required to settle the return in question.

 

971 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Haag v. Commonwealth


No. 2500 C.D. 1978


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


66 Pa. Commw. 249; 443 A.2d 888; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1233


March 5, 1982, Argued  
April 16, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County in the case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. William H. Haag, No. 76-5365-10-6.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of appellee, the Pennsylvania Secretary of Transportation, suspending appellant's license to operate a motor vehicle, pursuant to the provisions of 75 P.S. § 624.1(a), for refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was arrested and charged with driving under the influence of alcohol. When requested to take a breathalyzer test, appellant refused. Appellee, the Pennsylvania Secretary of Transportation, suspended appellant's motor vehicle operating privileges for six months, pursuant to Section 624.1(a) of the Vehicle Code of 1959, 75 P.S. § 624.1(a), for refusing to submit to a breathalyzer test. The lower court dismissed an appeal of the suspension. Appellant sought further review, contending that the Director of the Bureau of Traffic Safety's refusal to answer interrogatories was improper, and that Section 624.1(a) was unconstitutional because it contained an open-ended penalty. The court disagreed. The six-month suspension was set forth in appellee's standard schedule of suspensions. Suspension of licenses was an administrative function constitutionally delegated by the legislature that allowed appellee to determine periods of suspension. The refusal to answer interrogatories was proper because they related to issues irrelevant to the case, the Director's consideration of the suspension determination. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment approving the suspension of appellant driver's motor vehicle operating privileges for six months for refusal to submit to a breathalyzer test. The court concluded that the applicable statute was constitutional and that the refusal of an administrative officer to answer interrogatories was proper.

Fischer v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Public Welfare


No. 283 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


66 Pa. Commw. 70; 444 A.2d 774; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1203


September 16, 1981, Argued  
April 8, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued March 2, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original jurisdiction, in the case of Joanne Fischer et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Public Welfare et al.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections overruled and case certified for immediate appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner interested persons challenged 62 P.S. § 453, which restricted the use of public funds for abortions only if necessary to save the woman's life or if she was a rape or incest victim. Petitioners contended that the act violated freedom of religion, due process, privacy, and equal protection rights. Respondent Department of Public Welfare filed preliminary objections asserting that petitioners failed to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner interested persons brought an action to restrain implementation of 62 P.S. § 453 on the grounds that it violated freedom of religion, due process, privacy, and equal protection rights. The act restricted the use of public funds for abortions unless the procedure was necessary to save the woman's life or if she was a rape or incest victim. Respondent Department of Health further required the victim to report the offence within 72 hours of its occurrence. Respondent filed preliminary objections contending that petitioners failed to state a cause of action. Relying on the reasoning of a Supreme Court case, the court held that the act did not violate equal protection or due process rights. Because medical funding was not available to any woman needing an abortion to preserve her health, the court held that the act did not interfere with petitioners' religious freedoms. Absent direct and immediate injury, the court concluded that none of petitioners had standing to challenge respondent's reporting requirement, which was reasonable. Accordingly, the court sustained respondent's preliminary objections.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondent health department's preliminary objections against the constitutional attack on the restriction of public funds for abortions unless necessary to save the woman's life or if she was a rape or incest victim. The court held that none of petitioner interested persons had standing to challenge the reporting requirements and that the act did not violate their constitutional rights.

 

973 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Cavanaugh


No. 530 C.D. 1982


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


65 Pa. Commw. 620; 444 A.2d 1304; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1197


March 23, 1982, Heard  
April 6, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued April 19, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original jurisdiction in case of In Re: Objections to the Nomination Petition of James R. Cavanaugh for the 1982 Primary Election for the Democratic Nomination for the Office of Justice of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Previous order vacated. Petition granted. Secretary of Commonwealth directed not to certify name of candidate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought to have the objections for his nomination for public office that were filed by respondent objector dismissed because the requirement to obtain 100 valid signatures from each of five counties under 25 P.S. § 2872(b) imposed a substantial dilution of the voting influence of the electorate in more populous counties and violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner candidate sought to have the objections filed by respondent objector to his nomination for public office dismissed because the requirement to obtain 100 valid signatures from each of five counties under 25 P.S. § 2872(b) violated the equal protection clause under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that the geographic distribution requirement set forth in § 2872(b) was unconstitutional because it diluted the voting power of the electorate who resided in more populous counties in choosing an official who was to be elected by voters in an at-large state primary. The court found that the state's interest as implemented by § 2872(b) was not compelling, that an alternative method to achieve legitimate state aims was available, and § 2872(b) could not withstand strict scrutiny evaluation. The court dismissed the objections because the geographic distribution requirement for signatures on nomination petitions contained in § 2872(b) violated the "one person one vote" principle and was unconstitutional, and the nomination petitions at issue exceeded the requisite number of unchallenged signatures.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the objections of respondent objector and directed that petitioner candidate's name be included on the ballot because the geographic distribution requirement for signatures on nomination petitions for state offices violated the "one person one vote" principle contained in the Equal Protection Clause, and the disputed nomination petitions exceeded the requisite number of unchallenged signatures.

 

974 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


In re Act of October 7, 1976, etc.


No. 2479 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


66 Pa. Commw. 1; 444 A.2d 761; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1198


October 6, 1981, Argued  
April 5, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of the Fifty-Ninth Judicial District in case of In the Matter of the Salaries of County Officers, No. 78-1321, Elk County Branch, and No. 45 November Term, 1978, Cameron County Branch.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and affirmed in part. Case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant treasurer sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of the 59th Judicial District for Elk County (Pennsylvania), which held that appellee elected officials, who had received compensation increases under a state statute subsequently declared constitutionally inapplicable, did not have to reimburse for such unauthorized receipts and had to be paid at the rate established prior to the inapplicable statute.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted 16 P.S. § 11011-1 on October 7, 1976, which established compensation increases for certain county officials. One section of the statute provided that it would take effect immediately and would be applicable to each officer when permitted by the Constitution of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. Based on a Pennsylvania Supreme Court decision, appellant treasurer refused to sign any pay vouchers for the affected officials at either the new rates or the former pay rates. The trial court held that appellee elected officials need not return pay increases previously received, but must be paid at the rate established prior to enactment of 16 P.S. § 11011-1. The court reversed the trial court's decision as to the requirement that appellees need not return the pay increases received under 16 P.S. 11011-1. The court noted that appellees as elected officials had no inherent right to keep the additional compensation as they were only entitled to the salary expressly authorized by statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that appellee elected officials need not return pay increases made under the new legislation that authorized pay increases for elected officials. The court affirmed, however, the portion of the order that provided that appellees must be paid at the rate established prior to the new legislation. The court remanded for proceedings not inconsistent with its opinion.

Fink v. Board of Education


No. 1261 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


65 Pa. Commw. 320; 442 A.2d 837; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1135


June 1, 1981, Argued  
March 15, 1982, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued December 15, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Secretary of Education in case of Lloyd F. Fink v. Board of Education, Warren County School District, Teacher Tenure Appeal, No. 25-78.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school teacher appealed an order of the Secretary of the Department of Education (Pennsylvania), which affirmed respondent school board of education's decision to terminate petitioner's employment for conducting school prayer in violation of § 1122 of the Public School Code of 1949, 24 P.S. § 11-1122.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner school teacher who taught the fourth grade was fired by respondent school board of education for conducting prayer and reading from the Bible in class, despite numerous demands to cease and desist, which decision was affirmed by the secretary of education. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that petitioner's prayers said aloud were religious exercises that violated 24 P.S. § 1516.1, that when petitioner was praying and reading religious material he was utilizing the power, prestige, and influence conferred upon him by the state as a teacher, that petitioner's activities violated separation of church and state under the Establishment Clause, U.S. Const. amend. I, and that petitioner acted persistently, negligently, and willfully after numerous demands to cease and desist from conducting religious activities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment of the department of education which upheld the firing of petitioner school teacher because constitutional requirement for separation of church and state prevailed over petitioner's right to religious freedom, and petitioner acted persistently and willfully after receiving numerous demands to stop conducting religious activities in school.

 

976 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Hartford Acci. & Indem. Co. v. Insurance Comm'r of Commonwealth


No. 1184 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


65 Pa. Commw. 249; 442 A.2d 382; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1117


December 15, 1981, Argued  
March 10, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Insurance Commissioner of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, in the case of In Re: Philip V. Mattes, III v. Hartford Accident and Indemnity Company, No. R78-7-2.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance company sought review of an order of respondent insurance commissioner, which disapproved petitioner's use of sex as a classification basis for automobile insurance rate differentials, under the Casualty and Surety Rate Regulatory Act of 1947, 40 P.S. §§ 1181-1199. Intervenor insured contended that the sex-based distinction violated his state and federal constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insurance commissioner issued an order that disapproved petitioner insurance company's rating plan, which used sex as a classification basis for automobile insurance rate differentials under the Casualty and Surety Rate Regulatory Act of 1947, 40 P.S. §§ 1181-1199. Intervenor insured contended that the sex-based distinction violated his state and federal constitutional rights. The court affirmed respondent's order. The court stated that respondent had exercised his legislatively conferred function of evaluating the rating system by which risks were spread. The court concluded that the courts were not in a position to dispute respondent's conclusion that the use of sex as a rating classification was not a necessary pillar to the automobile insurance system. The court decided that respondent did not exceed his statutory authority but that he instead considered the actuarial factors and all other relevant factors within and outside the commonwealth to determine that sex-based auto insurance classification was inherently unfairly discriminatory because it failed to treat equals equally.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent insurance commissioner, which disapproved petitioner insurance company's use of sex as a classification basis for automobile rate differentials. The court held that respondent did not exceed his statutory authority when he determined that sex-based auto insurance classification was inherently unfairly discriminatory because it failed to treat equals equally.

Country Paradise Park, Inc. v. Sugarcreek


No. 513 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


65 Pa. Commw. 93; 441 A.2d 821; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1081


October 8, 1981, Submitted on Briefs  
March 2, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County in case of Township of Sugarcreek v. Country Paradise Park, Inc., No. 1980-0737 -- Civil.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County (Pennsylvania), which held that appellee township's amusement tax under the Local Tax Enabling Act was constitutional. 53 P.S. § 6901.


OVERVIEW: The trial court entered an order that held that appellee township's amusement tax under the Local Tax Enabling Act was constitutional. 53 P. S. § 6901. The trial court's order was affirmed. The court held that 53 P.S. § 6904 of the ordinance when read in conjunction with 53 P.S. § 6902, clearly defined who would be subject to the tax. The court held that appellant taxpayer operated a place of amusement for the public and charged an admission price. The record contained no evidence that other operations charged an admission price as required by the ordinance and no other enterprise met the categories defined in § 6904 of the ordinance and the court found no constitutional infirmity from the mere fact that appellant was the only operation taxed under the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order because the court found no constitutional infirmity from the mere fact that appellant taxpayer was the only operation taxed under the ordinance since appellant operated a place of amusement for the public and charged an admission price.

Bradley v. South Londonderry


No. 2088 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


64 Pa. Commw. 395; 440 A.2d 665; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1038


September 17, 1981, Submitted on Briefs  
February 3, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County in case of Township of South Londonderry, County of Lebanon, Pennsylvania v. Thomas G. Bradley and Mary L. Bradley, No. 21, 1978.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owner and user, sought review of a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County (Pennsylvania), for appellee township, in an action to enforce appellee's ordinances, South Londonderry, Pa., Junkyard Ordinance § 212 and South Londonderry, Pa., Zoning Ordinance § 508.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, property owner and user, had several unregistered vehicles exposed to the elements that had not been moved for four to ten years on appellant property owner's property. Appellee township contended appellants were in violation of South Londonderry, Pa., Junkyard Ordinance § 212 and South Londonderry, Pa., Zoning Ordinance § 508. The trial court determined that the vehicles were a nuisance in fact. The court affirmed and held that under 53 P.S. §§ 10101-11202, 65510-65512, appellee had the capacity to bring the action without an exhaustion of its statutory remedies and appellee's solicitor was authorized to bring such action. Section 212 was not invalid because it did not automatically make appellants' activities a nuisance in fact and there was sufficient evidence to prove that the activities were a nuisance in fact and violated the ordinances. The court also held that the term "junkyard" in §§ 212, 508 were not irreconcilable, the term "discarded" was not so vague as to make the ordinances unconstitutional, the issues of nuisance in fact were properly raised before the trial court, and the vehicles were not "stored," as proved by their condition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision for appellee township in an action to enforce appellee's junkyard and zoning ordinance against appellants, property owner and user, because appellee had the jurisdiction to enforce the ordinances, properly authorized the action taken against appellants, and there was sufficient evidence to support the decisions.

Marriott Corp. v. Bd. of Assessment Appeals


Nos. 1429 C.D. 1980, 650 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 622; 438 A.2d 1032; 1982 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 969


November 18, 1981, Argued; November 19, 1981, Argued  
January 6, 1982, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County in the case of Marriott Corporation v. Board of Assessment Appeals of Montgomery County, No. 76-18918. Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County in the case of Street Road Associates v. Bucks County Board of Assessment Appeals, No. 80-2885-11-5.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's petition for the allowance of real estate tax assessment. Appellant board of assessment sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which issued an order in mandamus directing appellant to grant appellee partnership a hearing on the value assessed to property.


OVERVIEW: In separate actions, the assessment values of the property of appellant corporation and appellee partnership were reduced on appeal to their respective county boards of assessment. However, both appellant corporation and appellee partnership remained dissatisfied and sought review of the assessments in the trial court. The trial court issued an order directing that appellant board of assessment grant appellee partnership a hearing on the value of its assessed property. The trial court denied appellant corporation's petition for the allowance of real estate tax appeals. Both county assessment boards sought review of the orders of the trial court. The court held that the amended section to the General Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5350, granted appellant corporation and appellee partnership automatic reviews of those assessments which were time-barred before the amendment became effective. Therefore appellant corporation was granted an appeal and appellee partnership's hearing was sustained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that denied appellant corporation's petition for the allowance of real estate tax assessment. The court affirmed the decision that issued an order of mandamus granting appellee partnership a hearing on the value assessed to its property. The amended section of the General Assessment Law granted automatic appeals of tax assessments even though appeals from them were otherwise time-barred before its effective date.

Marino v. Seneca Homes, Inc.


No. 845 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 534; 439 A.2d 1287; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2022


October 5, 1981, Argued  
December 31, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County in case of Daniel A. Marino, Jr. and Marilyn L. Marino, his wife v. Seneca Homes, Inc. and The Hempfield Township Municipal Authority and Duncan, Lagnese and Associates Inc. v. Michael Bove, t/a Bove Engineering Company v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Environmental Resources, No. 140 of 1979.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant engineering company challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee Department of Environmental Resources' demurrer that alleged the defense of sovereign immunity to appellant's complaint.


OVERVIEW: Appellant engineering company was brought into a suit in equity that sought to enjoin the discharging of sewer water on some property. Appellant filed an action against appellee Department of Environmental Resources, which alleged that appellee negligently approved the plans for the sewage plant. Appellee filed a demurrer that alleged the defense of sovereign immunity. After appellee's demurrer was sustained, appellant sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed and found that Act 152, 1978 Pa. Laws 788, reinstated the doctrine of sovereign immunity except for eight categories where immunity was waived. The court found that sovereign immunity did not lie for actions before September 28, 1978 and that it was a bar to actions which accrued after that date unless it was set out as an exception pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8522. The court found that the accrual of the original cause of action brought by the landowners was after September 28, 1978. The court found that the case against appellee was barred by sovereign immunity because the action was not in the exceptions. Finally, the court found that Act 152 was constitutional pursuant to Pa. Const. art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which sustained the demurrer of appellee Department of Environmental Resource raising the defense of sovereign immunity, because the cause of action accrued after the statute which reinstated sovereign immunity was enacted. The court also affirmed because the cause of action alleged was not one where sovereign immunity was waived and because the statute was constitutional.

 

982 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Scott v. Palmerton Area School Dist.


No. 3031 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 528; 439 A.2d 859; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1996


November 20, 1981, Argued  
December 30, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Carbon County in case of Joseph M. Scott et al. v. The Palmerton Area School District, No. 80 Equity 30.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Carbon County (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellee school district and sustained a motion to strike for lack of subject matter jurisdiction for failure to exercise a statutory remedy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers brought a class action suit in equity against appellee school district alleging that the occupational tax and assessment adopted by appellee be declared unconstitutional because it was discriminatory on the basis of sex and in violation of their state and federal constitutional rights. Appellants also alleged that the willful adoption of the tax violated their rights under the Civil Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Appellee filed a motion to strike for failure to exercise a statutory remedy, which was sustained by the trial court. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the order. The court held that appellants were required to avail themselves of their statutorily prescribed remedies notwithstanding the fact that a constitutional question may be present, unless the statutory remedies were inadequate. Appellants did not show that the statutory remedies available to them were inadequate. Therefore, appellants' cause of action was inadequate to invoke equitable jurisdiction. Although the Civil Rights Act authorized an equitable remedy, it did not confer equitable jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision to sustain appellee school district's motion to strike for lack of subject matter jurisdiction for failure to exercise a statutory remedy. Appellant taxpayers failed to pursue their statutory remedies in lieu of bringing a complaint under the Civil Rights Act; it was an improper proceeding and though the Civil Rights Act authorized an equitable remedy, it did not confer equitable jurisdiction.

Oknefski v. Workmen's Compensation Appeal Bd.


No. 2915 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 450; 439 A.2d 846; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 2003


October 8, 1981, Argued  
December 29, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board in case of Cindy Oknefski, deceased; Frederic Oknefski, widower v. Louisiana Pacific Corporation, No. A-79458.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widower sought review of an order of respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a referee's decision that denied him death benefits pursuant to the Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Act, 77 P.S. § 562.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner widower's wife died as a result of a work-related accident. Petitioner was denied death benefits by a referee, which denial was affirmed by respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board. Petitioner sought review and argued that 77 P.S. § 562 was discriminatory. Respondent wife's employer in turn argued that the disparate treatment of § 562 of widows and widowers was substantially related to important government interests. The court disagreed. The court found that the statute discriminated against a male surviving spouse. The court found the statute to be underinclusive because widowers who were capable of self-support were denied death benefits even though they were actually dependent upon their wives' income, while widows who were capable of self-support received death benefits if they established that they were living with their husbands at the time of their death or were actually dependent upon their husbands. Thus, the court found § 562 to be unconstitutionally discriminatory. The court reversed the order and remanded to respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board for a computation of death benefits to petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to respondent Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board for a computation of death benefits to petitioner widower. The court found the death benefit statute under the Pennsylvania Workmen's Compensation Act to be unconstitutionally discriminatory against a male surviving spouse, as it held a widower to a heavier burden of proof than a widow to be eligible for death benefits.

Robson v. Penn Hills School Dist.


No. 2905 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 250; 437 A.2d 1273; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1942


October 6, 1981, Argued  
December 16, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in the case of Jeffrey S. Robson, a minor, by Edwin G. Robson and Elizabeth L. Robson, parents and natural guardians and Edwin G. Robson and Elizabeth L. Robson, in their own right v. Penn Hills School District, No. C.D. 80-03949.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an injured child and his parents, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) granting appellee school district's motion for summary judgment and dismissing appellants' complaint on the ground that the allegations of the complaint were not within the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, former Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 5311.202(b)(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a minor and his parents, sued appellee school district after the minor appellant was struck in the eye by a pencil while at school. No teacher or other adult was present at the time of the injury. The trial court dismissed the action, finding it outside the purview of the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, former Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 5311.202(b)(3). The court affirmed, rejecting appellants' contention that appellee's negligent conduct was directly related to the care, custody, and control of real property. It would be a distortion of the language of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 5311.202(b)(3) to permit a lack of student supervision to fall within the scope of care, custody, and control of real property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entry of summary judgment for appellee school district, rejecting appellants' contention that their claim, grounded on the minor appellant's injury due to a pencil thrown by another student, fell within the statute permitting claims against political subdivisions.

Commonwealth v. Rich


Nos. 32 T.D. 1980, 34 T.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


63 Pa. Commw. 30; 437 A.2d 516; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1915


March 3, 1981, Argued  
December 1, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in the cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Allison Rich, Citation No. 77-06-57243; Darryl Edwards (a/k/a David Gemza) Citation No. 77-06-062364; Fred Vennell, Citation No. 77-06-054700; Linda Jackson and Ronald Kifer, Citation No. 77-06-57898 and Betty Jane Allsup, Citation No. 77-09-048506, and in cases of Paul Harris, Citation No. 78-06-11876; Robert Lee Bradfield, Citation No. 78-06-11878; Julia Rieman, Citation No. 78-09-09306; Robert Goldsmith, Citation No. 78-15-13536 and Joseph Steiner, Citation No. 78-24-07007, dated July 25, 1978.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth sought review of the decision entered by the Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which struck down as unconstitutional Philadelphia's Obscenity Ordinance, Philadelphia, Pa. Code ch. 10, § 1100 (1977).


OVERVIEW: Cases on appeal and transfer from municipal court were consolidated for review of the decision striking as unconstitutional the Philadelphia, Pa. Obscenity Ordinance (Ordinance), Pa. Code ch. 10 § 1100 (1977). The court found that the statute revealed neither an express nor implied legislative intention to prevent municipalities from enacting supplemental pornography legislation. When comparing the ordinance with the State Obscenity Act (Act), 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903, for inconsistencies or contradictions, the court disagreed with the lower court's holding that the ordinance was preempted because it conflicted materially with the Commonwealth's legislation, specifically with regard to the Act's jury trial provisions, since the ordinance prosecuted defendants in municipal court without a jury. The court found that defendants cited under the ordinance would suffer no constitutional deprivation since they had an absolute right to a jury trial in the Common Pleas Court, the second tier of the cities' two-tier prosecutorial system.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded the matter, concluding that there was no legislative intent to prevent municipalities from enacting supplemental pornography legislation, that the city ordinance did not conflict materially with the Commonwealth's legislation, and that the provision for trial by jury in the city's two-tier prosecutorial system removed any constitutional deprivation alleged in the municipal system.

Commonwealth v. 9 Mills Mechanical Slot Machines


No. 2061 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


62 Pa. Commw. 397; 437 A.2d 67; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1873


October 5, 1981, Submitted on Briefs  
November 16, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. 9 Mills Mechanical Slot Machines, 1 Bally Electronic Gambling Device and 1 Draw Poker Gambling Device; alleged owner, Miles Angelo, No. 3719 of 1979, In Rem.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania) ordering the confiscation and destruction of 11 gambling devices and the forfeiture of their monies under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5513(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged the seizure of his 11 gambling devices by police from an Elks Club event open to the public. The trial court held that the machines were unlawful gambling devices and ordered them destroyed and their money forfeited under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 5513(b). On appeal, the court affirmed. The court noted that even if the Gold Cup and Draw Poker machines were not gambling devices per se, they would still be subject to confiscation and forfeiture under § 5513(b).

987 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Kury v. Commonwealth


No. 273 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


62 Pa. Commw. 174; 435 A.2d 940; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1824


September 15, 1981, Argued  
October 9, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the State Ethics Commission in case of In Re: Franklin L. Kury, No. 81-001.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of the order from the State Ethics Commission (Pennsylvania), which prohibited petitioner from representing any client before the Public Utility Commission (PUC), or performing certain other enumerated functions before the PUC, for one year after his term as a member of the state senate had expired.


OVERVIEW: Respondent State Ethics Commission issued an opinion to petitioner attorney prohibiting him from representing any client before the State Senate or the Public Utility Commission (PUC), or perform certain other enumerated functions before those bodies, for one year after the expiration of his term as a State Senator. Petitioner sought review of respondent's order as it applied to his activities before the PUC. The court held that under the common and approved usage of the term "associated", petitioner was not associated with the PUC solely by reason of his chairmanship of a legislative committee that dealt with the PUC among other agencies, bureaus, and commissions. Further, pursuant to case law precedents, the Ethics Act, 65 P.S. § 403(e), was an impermissible intrusion by the legislature into an area reserved by the Constitution to the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania. Since petitioner was a member of the bar, the Supreme Court regulated his conduct in the rendering of professional legal services to clients with business before the PUC. Accordingly, respondent's order was reversed insofar as it attempted to regulate petitioner's representation of clients before the PUC.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order prohibiting petitioner attorney from representing clients before the Public Utility Commission (PUC) for one year after his term as a State Senator because petitioner was not associated with the PUC solely by reason of his chairmanship of a legislative committee that dealt with the PUC. Further, it was the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania which had the authority to regulate petitioner's conduct before the PUC.

Lancaster Yellow Cab & Baggage, Inc. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 1796 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


61 Pa. Commw. 160; 432 A.2d 1143; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1624


June 4, 1981, Argued  
July 27, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission in the case of In Re: Application of Carlos Ortiz, Jr. and William Rodrigues, co-partners, t/d/b/a Bluebird Taxi Company, for the right to begin to transport as a common carrier, by motor vehicle, persons, upon call or demand, in the City of Lancaster, Lancaster County, and within an airline distance of ten (10) statute miles of the limits of said city, No. A.101665.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent public utility commission adopted a decision of an administrative law judge granting applicants' request for a certificate of public convenience to engage in a taxicab business. Petitioner cab companies appealed, contending that the evidence as to the applicants' technical and financial capacity to perform the proposed services and the need for the proposed services was insufficient.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner cab companies sought review of an order from respondent public utility commission adopting the decision of an administrative law judge granting applicants' request for a certificate of public convenience to engage in a taxicab business subject to certain limitations. The court affirmed and held that there was sufficient evidence supporting the findings in favor of the applicants. The evidence showed that they had driven taxicabs in other cities; one of the applicants had been a policeman and had worked as a prison guard and for a grocery store. The applicants also were fluent in English and Spanish. In addition, they already had two motor vehicles and one of the applicants owned real estate for which he received monthly rent. In addition, the record showed that there was sufficient evidence to establish that the proposed taxi services were necessary. Witnesses testified that the current services provided were insufficient and that there was a lack of service to the Spanish-speaking community. Accordingly, the court held that the record supported respondent board's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that the evidence was sufficient to support respondent public utility commission's grant of a certificate of public convenience to applicants to engage in a taxicab business. The evidence established that the applicants were qualified technically and financially to perform the business. In addition, the proposed business was needed to serve the public because the existing cab services were insufficient.

Kacar, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 287 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


60 Pa. Commw. 582; 432 A.2d 310; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1601


March 4, 1981, Argued  
July 17, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County in case of Kacar, Inc. and Pasquale and Josephine Dimilio v. The Zoning Hearing Board of the City of Allentown, No. 79-C-1846.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, adult business and business owners, sought review of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania) judgment affirming the denial of a constitutional challenge to Allentown, Pa., Ordinance 12260, which established limits on locations of adult businesses. The judgment also upheld appellee zoning board's denial of a special exception permit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant adult business was located in an area zoned for business use. Appellants, adult business and business owners, were cited for violating Allentown, Pa., Ordinance 12260, and their application for a special exception permit was denied by appellee zoning board. The trial court upheld appellee's denial of a special exception permit and found that the ordinance did not violate the constitution. The court held that the ordinance satisfied the applicable standards set forth in recent United States Supreme Court decisions. The government action furthered a substantial and legitimate state interest without unnecessarily infringing on individual rights. The ordinance did not regulate expression or speech; it regulated economic exploitation of certain films and books. Its purpose was to prevent the deterioration of neighborhoods with minimal infringement of constitutional guarantees. The court further held that the ordinance was not improperly applied to appellant adult business, that it was not exclusionary, and that it did not impermissibly delegate legislative powers. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of appellee zoning board in the constitutional challenge to an adult business location ordinance. The court also upheld the denial of a special exception permit to appellants, adult business and business owners. The ordinance represented a constitutional exercise of zoning power.

Philadelphia Facilities Management Corp. v. Biester


No. 616 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


60 Pa. Commw. 366; 431 A.2d 1123; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1650


December 9, 1980, Argued  
July 8, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original jurisdiction in the case of Philadelphia Facilities Management Corporation, in its own right and on behalf of the Philadelphia Gas Works and all of its rate-paying customers, and Philadelphia Gas Works, in its own right and on behalf of all of its rate-paying customers v. The Honorable Edward G. Biester, Attorney General for the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and The Honorable Howard A. Cohen, Secretary of Revenue for the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and The Honorable Robert E. Casey, State Treasurer for the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer sustained. Complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, management corporation and gas company, brought an action against defendant state to declare that the Utility Service Tenants Rights Act, 68 P.S. §§ 399.1 et seq., which sought to protect residential tenants from discontinuance of utility service caused by the failure of the landlord ratepayer to pay the bill, violated of the federal and state constitutions, and to enjoin the act's enforcement.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, management corporation and gas company, brought an action on behalf of utility rate payers against defendant state to declare that Utility Service Tenants Rights Act, 68 P.S. §§ 399.1 et seq., which sought to protect residential tenants from discontinuance of utility service caused by the failure of the landlord ratepayer to pay the bill, violated of the federal and state constitutions. The court sustained defendant's preliminary objections and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint finding that plaintiff gas company had no legal existence, and thus no capacity to be a party to the suit, and plaintiff management corporation, as a private corporation, had no standing to press four of the constitutional challenges because it was not directly or substantially affected in a legal sense. The court also found that, even if plaintiff management corporation had standing to challenge the statute as violative of the right of local home rule, no cause of action was stated on that claim because the statute that granted home rule rights did not deprive the state legislature of the power to regulate the proprietary, private businesses of home rule cities.


OUTCOME: Claim of plaintiffs, management corporation and gas company, against defendant state asserting that the Utility Service Tenants Rights Act, which protected residential tenants from discontinuance of utility service, violated the federal and state constitutions was dismissed for lack of standing because plaintiff gas company was not a legal entity and plaintiff management corporation was not directly affected.

 

992 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Wilson v. Western Pennsylvania Water Co.


No. 1686 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


60 Pa. Commw. 312; 430 A.2d 1247; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1574


May 4, 1981, Argued  
July 2, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County in the case of Henry L. Wilson and Geraldine L. Wilson, his wife; and James A. Podobnik and Kathleen B. Podobnik, his wife v. Western Pennsylvania Water Company, No. 7611, in Equity.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land owners sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County (Pennsylvania) which dismissed appellants' complaint in equity relative to the condemnation of easements by appellee water company for installation of a water line across appellants' properties.


OVERVIEW: Appellant land owners challenged appellee water company's condemnation resolution, which allowed appellee easements to place water lines through appellants' properties. The trial court interpreted an earlier trial court ruling as having dismissed appellants' equity complaint. Appellants contended that they were denied due process of law when appellee filed a motion to transfer decision on the rule without notifying appellants. The court decided to look past procedural flaws, and made a determination on the merits of the case, reversing the order of dismissal and remanding the matter. The court found that the Business Corporation Law, 15 P.S. § 3241, provided a procedure by which the power to condemn may be challenged. Because it would be unconstitutional to deny appellants the right to challenge the taking of their land, appellants' proper remedy was through an action in equity. The matter was remanded to allow appellants a trial.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order dismissing appellant land owners' complaint in equity relative to the condemnation of easements by appellee water company for installation of a water line across appellants' properties was reversed. Although statutes did not provide for a challenge to condemnation, constitutional principles required that appellants be allowed to challenge the condemnation. Thus, appellants' action in equity was allowed to proceed.

 

993 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Gill v. Commonwealth


No. 399 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


60 Pa. Commw. 247; 431 A.2d 376; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1584


April 8, 1981, Submitted on Briefs  
June 30, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. James P. Gill, Jr., No. SA 1296 of 1978.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the suspension of his driver's license for 65 days, under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1539, because he had accumulated 13 points in less than two years.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver had accumulated a total of 13 points in less than two years. Accordingly, his license was ordered suspended for 65 days by the secretary of transportation, under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1539. The lower court affirmed. Appellant did not challenge any of the convictions that led to his total point accumulation, but challenged the constitutionality of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1550(c) because it deprived him of an operator's license without a fair hearing by limiting the issue on appeal to the identity of the motorist. The court affirmed, holding that appellant's contentions were utterly devoid of merit. Section 1550(c) was not the source of power by which appellant's operator's privilege was ordered suspended. It was suspended under § 1539. In addition, § 1550(c) extended the scope of the judicial hearing to the identity of the motorist and to the issue of whether the privilege was subject to the recall, suspension, cancellation, or revocation. Moreover, under § 1550(c), the lower court was not limited to a determination of the identity of the licensee, but had to hear evidence upon, and reach the merits of the suspension.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's affirmance of appellant driver's license suspension for 65 days because he had accumulated 13 points in less than two years. The court held that appellant's constitutional arguments were utterly devoid of merit because he attacked a different statute than the one he was suspended under.

 

994 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Leasoff v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp.


No. 1131 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


59 Pa. Commw. 45; 428 A.2d 783; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1409


March 2, 1981, Argued  
April 28, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County in case of Ernest Leasoff v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, No. 79-6444-11-2.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured person sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his action in trespass against respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, as barred by the two-year statute of limitation pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5524(2).


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured person sought review of an order that dismissed his action in trespass against respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation. The order declared that appellant's action, which alleged that respondent negligently failed to repair a pothole, was barred by the statute of limitation under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5524(2). Appellant contended that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court's retroactive abrogation of the governmental defense of sovereign immunity had revived his cause of action against respondent. Specifically, he claimed that respondent's prior immunity was analogous to situations in which a tortfeasor concealed his victim's cause of action, thus tolling the statute of limitation until the cause of action was discovered. The court affirmed and held that pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5522, the statute of limitations was maintained in cases where the legislature had waived sovereign immunity. The court found that to permit an exception to the statute of limitations would have injected life into countless claims against respondent concerning evidence which was unavailable and subjected respondent to numerous fraudulent claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which dismissed appellant injured person's action in trespass against respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, as barred by the two-year statute of limitation. The court held that a retroactive abrogation of sovereign immunity did not revive cases that were previously barred by the statute of limitations.

 

995 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Haines v. Commonwealth


No. 556 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 640; 428 A.2d 759; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1421


February 3, 1981, Argued  
April 24, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the State Civil Service Commission in case of State Civil Service Commission v. Charles A. Haines, Jr., Appeal No. 2785.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed an order from the State Civil Service Commission (Pennsylvania), which dismissed petitioner from his probationary employee position because of political activity, pursuant to the Civil Service Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.904, and Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.906.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee was in a probationary civil service position when he subsequently filed for nomination to political office. Petitioner was dismissed for violating the proscription against political activity for employees in classified service, under the Civil Service Act (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.904, and removed pursuant to the Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.906. The court affirmed. Probationary employees were within the meaning of classified service as defined by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.3(d)(1), for purposes of prohibitions found in § 741.904. The court rejected petitioner's assertion that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, § 741.603(a) was a penal statute that provided the exclusive criteria for removal of probationary employees, because such a construction could create absurd results with respect to other statutes permitting the removal of probationary employees. Section 904 was not unconstitutionally vague when read in conjunction with the Act as a whole. Nor did the Act create unconstitutional classifications when there was a legitimate interest in requiring persons to choose between civil service or a political career.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of petitioner employee from his probationary civil service position for political activity under the Civil Service Act. Probationary employees were classified service subjecting them to removal under statutes that were not penal in nature, nor unconstitutionally vague. Finally, there was no unconstitutional classification because there was a legitimate interest in treating classified service differently.

Christ United Methodist Church v. Bethel Park


No. 141 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 610; 428 A.2d 745; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1388


February 2, 1981, Argued  
April 22, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County in the case of Christ United Methodist Church and the Wesley Institute, Inc. v. Borough of Bethel Park, No. SA 974 of 1978 and in the case of Christ United Methodist Church and the Wesley Institute, Inc. v. Zoning Hearing Board of the Municipality of Bethel Park, No. SA 1074 of 1978.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipality challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee group home's challenges to the denial of an occupancy permit and that held appellant's zoning ordinance either unconstitutionally excluded the use of property for group homes or was invalid due to unreasonable minimum lost size requirements.


OVERVIEW: Appellee group home purchased land within appellant municipality to operate a group home for adjudicated delinquent male juveniles. Appellee was denied an occupancy permit because a group home was an institutional house and the subject property failed to meet the area requirements for that use pursuant to appellant's zoning ordinance. The trial court granted appellee's challenge to the denial of the permit, and found that the ordinance unconstitutionally excluded the use of property within the municipality for group homes, and imposed unreasonable lot size requirements for that use. The appellate court held that the definition of an institutional house included group homes, and that the ordinance unconstitutionally de facto excluded group homes because it could not ignore the actual exclusion of group homes from the municipality. The minimum lot size requirement was the invalidating feature because it required a minimum lot size much larger than that required for its proposed use, and appellant did not present evidence as to the public interest to be protected by those requirements. The appellate court remanded for a more definitive order granting appellee relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted appellee group home's challenges to appellant municipality's denial of a of an occupancy permit. A zoning ordinance that excluded institutional houses, which included group homes, by imposing unduly restrictive minimum lot sizes was an unconstitutional de facto exclusion. The court remanded for a more definitive order granting appellee relief.

Hopewell Township Bd. of Supervisors v. Golla


No. 2575 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 572; 428 A.2d 701; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1391


September 10, 1980 Argued  
April 21, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued December 10, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County in case of Edward B. Golla and Elizabeth M. Golla, his wife, and Edward C. Golla, Eric N. Golla and Gregory E. Golla, minors, by Edward B. Golla, their guardian v. Hopewell Township Board of Supervisors and Hopewell Township Zoning Hearing Board, No. 78-S-2922.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, township supervisors and township zoning board, sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania) that found certain agricultural zone provisions of a township zoning ordinance were unconstitutional and that the zoning ordinance regulated the subdivision of land in violation of the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 P.S. § 10107(21).


OVERVIEW: After appellee landowners conveyed a portion of their agricultural property to their sons and contemplated additional partitioning into tract of more than 10 acres, appellant township supervisors indicated appellees would be prosecuted under a township zoning ordinance. Appellant township zoning board found the ordinance valid. However, the trial court found that the ordinance was invalid because it violated the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code (MPC), 53 P.S. § 10107(21), and was not rationally related to the preservation of agricultural land. Appellants sought review and argued that the ordinance was valid and that the trial court failed to make specific findings required by the MPC, 53 P.S. § 11011, for a declaration that a zoning ordinance was invalid. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the zoning ordinance violated § 10107(21) as that it was a regulation of the subdivision of land by the township, had no rational relationship to its avowed purpose of preserving prime agricultural land, and § 11011 was an unconstitutional transgression of the separation of legislative and judicial powers.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the township zoning ordinance violated the subdivision enabling provision of the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code (MPC), was not rationally related its purpose of preserving agricultural land, and the MPC's legislative restriction on judicial review of zoning ordinances violated the constitutional separation of powers.

S. H. Goss, Inc. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Agriculture


Nos. 397 C.D. 1981, 398 C.D. 1981


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 516; 428 A.2d 731; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1380


February 5, 1981, Argued  
April 15, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Filed and Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from the Order of the Department of Agriculture in cases of In Re: S.H. Goss, Inc., No. 78-1, and In Re: Central Chemical Corporation, No. 78-2.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner manufacturers sought review of an order from respondent Department of Agriculture (Pennsylvania), which assessed penalties under 3 P.S. § 68.7 for deficiencies in their fertilizers. Petitioners claimed they were denied due process by the faulty sampling methods and the investigational allowances used by respondent. They also challenged the sufficiency of the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner manufacturers were fined 125 penalties pursuant to 3 P.S. § 68.7 after testing and analysis by the agriculture secretary showed their fertilizer was deficient in certain ingredients it was guaranteed to contain. Petitioners challenged the order from respondent Department of Agriculture contending it violated their due process rights and was not supported by sufficient evidence. On appeal, the court found no constitutional infirmity in respondent's failure to notify petitioners when samples of the fertilizers were taken because consumers located far from petitioners often made such requests. The court rejected petitioners' attack on the sampling method used by respondent, because it was reasonably related to the purpose of protecting consumers. Although the court found petitioners' claim persuasive that the investigational allowance was calculated for a different type of fertilizer, the court found that it was not so unreasonable as to violate due process. Holding that the evidence was sufficient, the court affirmed the orders.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders by respondent agriculture department assessing penalties against petitioner manufacturers because testing showed that their fertilizer was deficient of ingredients it was labelled to contain. The court found that sampling methods and investigational allowances used were not so unreasonable to violate due process and that the evidence was sufficient to support the orders.

City Council Members v. Consumers Education & Protective Asso.


No. 1329 C.D. 1980


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 444; 428 A.2d 711; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1386


November 18, 1980, Argued  
April 13, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County in the case of Consumers Education and Protective Association; Consumer Party; Tenant Action Group; Puerto Rican Alliance; Residents' Coalition; Lee Frissell, Executive Director, Consumers Education and Protective Association; Max Weiner, Chairperson, Consumer Party; John Brickhouse; Maisha Jackson; Eva Gladstein; Juan Ramos, President, Puerto Rican Alliance; Ralph Wynder; Father Joseph Kakalec, President, Philadelphia Council of Neighborhood Organizations; Shaheed Abdul-Haqq, and Ralph Goldstein v. George X. Schwartz, President of the City Council of Philadelphia; James J. Tayoun; Anna Cibotti Verna; Lucien E. Blackwell; John F. Street; Joan Krajewski; Harry P. Jannotti; Joseph E. Coleman; Louis C. Johanson; Brian O'Neill; Al Pearlman; Francis Rafferty; Augusta Clark; John Anderson; David Cohen; and Joan Specter, all members of the Philadelphia City Council; William J. Green, Mayor of the City of Philadelphia; Thomas Leonard, Controller of the City of Philadelphia; Joseph Sullivan; Edgar C. Campbell, Sr., Clerk of Quarter Sessions; Ronald R. Donatucci, Register of Wills of the City of Philadelphia; Margaret [***2] Tartaglione; Eugene E. J. Maier; John Kane, Philadelphia City Commissioners; Edward G. Rendell, District Attorney of Philadelphia; Gay P. Gervin, Treasurer of the City of Philadelphia; and G. Edward DeSeve, Director of Finance of the City of Philadelphia, No. 591, January Term, 1980, In Equity.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city officials challenged the order of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which enjoined the implementation of the pay scale for 26 elected city offices.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city council, by a unanimous vote of those members present, passed a bill that raised the salary of numerous appellant city officials. Appellees, citizens and residents of appellant city and organizations, filed a suit in equity to enjoin the implementation of the pay scale for all 26 elected offices. The trial court entered a decree nisi enjoining the payment, acceptance or receipt of any increase in salary or benefits arising from the ordinance. The court affirmed the order in part, holding that appellant sheriff, city commissioners, register of wills, and district attorney, were dependent on appellant city's legislative branch for compensation. The court reversed as to the other officials, holding that appellant city council properly maintained and shouldered the responsibility for compensation of the council members, mayor, city controller, clerk of court of quarter sessions, oyer and terminer, and general jail delivery. The court held that, as to these officials, the ordinance did not violate the Pennsylvania Constitution, that there were no statutory prohibitions to the ordinance, and that common law principles of conflicts of interests were pre-empted by statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order as it applied to the salaries of several appellants: sheriff, city commissioners, register of wills, and district attorney, holding that appellant city commission could not raise the salary of those officials. The court reversed the order as it applied to mayor, city controller, clerk of the court of quarter sessions, oyer and terminer and general jail delivery, and all members of the city council.

 

1000 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Fumo v. Commonwealth, Ins. Dep't


No. 1922 C.D. 1979


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


58 Pa. Commw. 392; 427 A.2d 1259; 1981 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1333


February 6, 1981, Argued  
April 9, 1981, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commissioner of Insurance in case of In Re: Vincent E. Fumo, a/k/a Vincent Fumo, Docket No. A79-2-15.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee appealed an order from the Commissioner of the Pennsylvania Department of Insurance that revoked petitioner's licenses to act as an insurance agent or broker, pursuant to the Insurance Commissioner's power under the Insurance Department Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §§ 233(a), and 252, and 31 Pa. Code § 33.7(a) and (b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner licensee was convicted of federal crimes, and almost three years later had his licenses to act as an insurance agent and broker revoked based upon those convictions. The court affirmed. There was no impermissible commingling of prosecutorial and adjudicative powers when the commissioner of insurance initiated revocation proceedings against petitioner and then ultimately decided to revoke petitioner's licenses. Further petitioner failed to show actual bias, thus preliminary inquiries into the probable cause to revoke did not raise a risk of prejudice sufficient to taint the revocation after an adversarial hearing. The Insurance Department Act (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §§ 233(a), and 252, which gave the commissioner discretion to revoke a license were not unconstitutionally vague when effective regulations, 31 Pa. Code § 33.7(a) and (b), adequately clarified the Act's language. There was no abuse of discretion in revoking petitioner's licenses solely upon his criminal convictions. 31 Pa. Code § 33.7(b). Finally, laches did not bar the revocation proceedings when no prejudice to petitioner was established by the three-year delay.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that revoked petitioner licensee's insurance agent and broker licenses pursuant to the Insurance Department Act (Act). There was no improper commingling of functions between regulatory and adversarial, the Act was not unconstitutionally vague when clarified by regulations, laches did not bar the revocation proceedings, and there was no abuse of discretion in revoking petitioner's licenses.
